==PAGE:4==
Summary
This dissertation investigates emotion recognition based on physiological signals from wearable devices.
In particular, we consider the problem of using the physiological signal of the blood-volume-pulse in the
presence of noise and analyse various approaches for diminishing the effect of the noise in relation to
feature extraction. Application-wise we explore different approaches to predictive emotion recognition
using data from a pilot-study, while evaluating which physiological signal features are of importance.
We start by studying the relevant literature to get an overview of the current field of research, estab-
lishing the theoretical foundation as well as assessing domain specific elements of emotion recognition.
Analysing the blood-volume-pulse in the presence of noise, we are able to develop a framework of
simple criteria for which it is feasible to use the physiological signal of the blood-volume-pulse based
upon the level of the signal-to-noise-ratio. We also devise a novel feature extraction method for the
blood-volume-pulse when in the presence of noise to improve the reliability of the pre-specified features.
This in conclusion outperforms that of standard 4ŌĆÖth order Butterworth bandpass filter with cut-off
frequencies of 1Hz and 8Hz on average, for a subset of time as well as frequency features.
Finally, upon pre-processing the data and describing the relevant models, we are able to expand
on the problem of predictive emotion recognition using the data from the pilot-study. We here assess
various approaches and models with the development of a random forest producing superior perfor-
mance to that of an established baseline. We find that emotion recognition using wearable devices is
characterised by the sensitivity of physiological signals causing high inter-participant variability leading
to di’¼Ćiculties in obtaining robust models with satisfactory generalisable properties. We analyse the
features of importance and find that these differ across domains and approaches, alongside the fact that
the features of the physiological signals of the heart-rate as well as the phasic component of the electro-
dermal activity are the most prominent. We also consider the incorporation of the blood-volume-pulse
using the designed framework, although with no success due to the poor quality of the signals.
We view the results in the interdisciplinary perspective of performing real-time classification of
obsessive-compulsive-disorder linked anxiety.

Notation
A note on the mathematical notation used throughout the dissertation. The appropriate notation will
in general be defined when necessary, although we shall here attempt to collect and present the most
fundamental notation in order to supply an overview. Per usual, characters such as a, ┬Ą, ŽĆ and the
like will denote scalars, while boldface characters exempli gratia x and ┬Ą will typically represent vec-
tors, which unless stated otherwise are column vectors. Furthermore, boldface characters which are
upper-case as in the case of ╬Ż will usually denote a matrix. The transpose of a vector or a matrix
will be indicated by a superscript T, hence xT is a row vector. We denote stochastic variables by
lower-case characters both due to convenience and clarity, as probability density functions are usually
written by conflating the argument with its corresponding scalar. However, we will at times also use
upper-case letters such as A, B and X to emphasise the fact that these are indeed stochastic vari-
ables. The letter i denotes the imaginary unit, if not in the context of an index. Lastly, a character
with a circumflex accent such as for instance ╦åx or ╦å┬Ą denotes either a prediction or an estimated quantity.
Indexing is generally done by subscripts such that xi is the iŌĆÖth element of a vector x or a sequence,
although we will use superscripts if deemed necessary to establish clarity. Likewise a vector with a
subscript xi indicates the iŌĆÖth vector, while xij is the element in row i and column j of the matrix X.
Note that; probability density function, probability distribution and distribution appear interchangeably.
We assert probability mass/density functions by p and q. These will in general be written without
writing the specific stochastic variable as a subscript due to this being apparent from its arguments,
hence for example writing p(x) instead of px(x). Moments will be written with the distribution or
stochastic variable as a subscript to indicate that the moment is with respect to this distribution or the
(marginal) probability mass/density function of the stochastic variable if deemed necessary to maintain
coherence, and without if the appropriate probability distribution is in fact clear. The gradient and
Hessian will likewise be written with a subscript explicitly indicating the variable for which to take the
derivative, expect if there is no ambiguity then we will indeed omit this. Conditional statements are rep-
resented by a | such that for instance p(A|B) is a conditional probability mass/density function, while
p(A, B) is a joint probability mass/density function. For stating that a stochastic variable follows or is
a sample from a specific probability distribution we will exempli gratia write x Ōł╝N(0, 1) if x follows
or is a sample from the standard Gaussian distribution. A convergence statement is denoted with ŌåÆ,
with the type of convergence stated unless this is unambiguous from the frame of reference. Function
declarations also use this notation, although this will inevitable be evident from the context. A closed
interval between a and b is denoted by [a, b] with the corresponding open interval being ]a, b[. Lastly,
A Ō¬░0 states that the matrix is positive semi-definite and det(A) denotes the matrix determinant.
Special notation includes; o(x2), which denotes little-o notation meaning the set of functions of x
that goes to zero more rapid than x2.
The character Ōłćdenotes both the gradient and the back-
wards difference operator, although this will indisputably be distinguishable from the context, while
the notation Ōłćd means that the operator is being applied d times. The character ŌŖÖdenotes element-wise
multiplication, ŌŖśdenotes element-wise division and xŌŚ”1/2 denotes the element-wise power (here to 1/2).
The natural numbers N do not include zero unless stated otherwise; by for instance a subscript N0.

==PAGE:10==
CHAPTER 1
Introduction
Obsessive-compulsive-disorder better known by its abbreviation OCD is a mental health disorder often
associated with individuals having an extreme fear of contact with perceived contamination as well as
an excessive concern for perfectionism, albeit it being far more extensive than this. The aforementioned
is as the name suggests, a psychological disorder which pertains to obsessions and compulsions in a
more general setting. Obsessions refer to the repeated occurrences of intrusive thoughts and impulses
which yield intense distress and anxiety, thus inhibiting a person of accomplishing activities in relation
to their core values. Compulsions attribute themselves to the recurring behaviours and thoughts that
a person adheres to in order to avail oneself of obsessions despite being a short-term solution, hence
the reason for being recurring. Likewise, it produces a discrepancy between a personŌĆÖs activities and
their pursuit of core values [60]. The treatment for obsessive-compulsive-disorder includes cognitive
behavioral therapy (CBT) and medicine such as serotonin reuptake inhibitor antidepressant medicine,
for instance escitalopram [60]. The prior constitutes exempli gratia exposure and response prevention
(ERP), which is composed of exposure to initialise an obsession and then response prevention to refrain
from acting upon this with compulsions [60]. This mental health disorder equally affects people across
age, sex and ethnicity, hence also the likes of children [60]. However, children may be particularly
vulnerable to obsessive-compulsive-disorder as the method of treatment id est exposure and response
prevention, ought to be extraordinarily di’¼Ćicult for a child to practise outside of therapy and thus
requires the need for parental guidance and interaction. This can be assisted by the use of wearable
devices such as for instance the Empatica E4 for classifying when a child experiences obsessions and
compulsions in real-time. This is done to enable the informing of parents to assist in response prevention
as established in therapy rather than alleviating the compulsive behaviour, which may otherwise be the
case [24]. We therefore propose the use and development of state-of-the-art methods within the field of
emotion recognition using physiological signals from wearable devices. Here exploiting machine learning
models for classifying emotional states accompanying obsessions within children and adolescents, hereby
anticipating to mitigate the potential risks associated with adhering to compulsive behaviour [24].
Recent work within the field of research has shown the possibilities of such methods [3, 12, 125].
However, despite this there is still several complications. One problem is how to use the physiological
signal of the blood-volume-pulse as this signal is particularly prone to noise. To address this problem
requires the need for additional research due to the limitation on relevant literature [12, 144].
Preliminary studies thus constitute a necessity prior to such real-time classifications being feasible.
In this dissertation we therefore investigate a number of preliminary studies relating to this research.
This is partly done by using data from a pilot-study completed prior to that of a clinical trial.
This dissertation now yields itself to the following research questions;
R1 To what extent is it possible to develop a machine learning model for emotion recognition based
on physiological signals from the Empatica E4?
R2 Which physiological signal features are of importance for emotion recognition?
R3 How can we pre-process and incorporate the blood-volume-pulse?
==PAGE:11==
1 Introduction
2
This dissertation takes the following structure to ensure a thorough inspection of the problem.
Literature Review: We begin by providing a thorough review of the relevant literature within the
respective field of research in order to account for the approach of the dissertation.
Preliminaries: We subsequently introduce and present the preliminary theory necessary to acquire a
comprehensive and meaningful understanding of the subsequent chapters. We therefore establish
essential concepts in both probability theory, statistics, signal-processing and machine learning.
Data: We provide a concise description of the data, the measurement device as well as the experimental
setup for the acquisition of data.
Pre-Processing: We here give a presentation of the relevant pre-processing procedure.
Methods: Here a presentation of the static predictive models, which we will utilise throughout the
dissertation to perform emotion recognition using the physiological signals from the Empatica E4.
Results: We present the results of the analysis for the blood-volume-pulse and the emotion recognition.
Discussion: We discuss the results in relation to the initial research questions.
Conclusion: Finally, we conclude upon the results in regards to the original research questions and
provide additional remarks and perspectives concerning future work and learning outcomes.
Appendices: We here provide subsidiary matters as well as present additional information regarding
the relevant theory and concepts within the mathematical subjects associated.
The reader is expected to have basic knowledge of probability theory, statistics and algorithms, alongside
a notable level of maturity within mathematics in a more general aspect.
==PAGE:12==
CHAPTER 2
Literature Review
To what extent is it possible to exploit the combination of machine learning algorithms and physiological
signals from wearable devices to attain feasible artificial intelligence solutions within the psychiatry?
And what are some of the current challenges?
We will here review the present scientific literature
available within the respective field of research, constituting a foundation for the dissertation.
We structure the review thematically by presenting central recurring themes present in the literature.
First, we provide an overview of the field of emotion recognition, we then review available open-source
data-sets. We proceed by investigating more technical aspects of the literature with regards to machine
learning algorithms and the use of physiological signals, while we also assess the extent to which the
literature employs artificial intelligence for different mental health disorders, finishing with a short con-
clusion. We commence the review by providing a short overview of emotion recognition.
Overview:
Emotions constitute an essential part of human-living and has done so throughout the history of
mankind.
From the way we interact with one another to our perception of reality, the impact of
emotions is omnipresent. Despite this, the definition of emotions is contentious and has prevailed the
community of psychology, which has lead to various attempts at quantifying emotions [143]. In recent
years, prominent research within machine learning, large amounts of available data as well as rapid
advancements within computational capacity has lead the way of modern data driven emotion recogni-
tion. While previous research has been based on uni-modal approaches using for instance physiological
signals, facial expressions and speech, present development in emotion recognition is governed by more
comprehensive multi-modal approaches to allow for complex problem solving and better results [143].
Data-sets:
The field of research has given rise to multiple multi-modal open-source data-sets with some of the
most well-known being; WESAD [129], K-EmoCon [102], and AMIGOS [90]. Some scholars such as for in-
stance the aforementioned makes use of self-report questionnaires to internally evaluate the perception
of emotions on a personal level, with the most common self-report questionnaire being PANAS [149],
while a combination of different self-report questionnaires also being a viable option, evident in WESAD.
This approach has the advantage of taking grounds in previous use and research of relevant self-report
questionnaires as well as being easy to implement and complete by participants as argued by [90], al-
though it has the disadvantage of participants conceivably not being true to oneselves. Other scholars,
including K-EmoCon and AMIGOS, differ by offering a more comprehensive assessment of the ground-truth
by combining self-report questionnaires with external ratings of their emotional state from video, thus
espousing a more dynamic and complete perspective based upon this approach as argued by [90, 102],
while further allowing for the analysis of the correspondence between internal and external assessments.
There is indeed also no customary approach for eliciting the desired emotional state within participants,
albeit two schools of thoughts being present in the literature; the first being that the participants en-
gage in an activity, this may for instance be a debate such as it is the case in K-EmoCon. This also
permits the development of the experiment to comprise groups, enabling the analysis of synchrony be-
tween participants (see for instance [76]). The second being a selection of specific videos shown to the
participants to induce the correct stimuli such to obtain the desired emotional state, which is the case
==PAGE:13==
2 Literature Review
4
for AMIGOS. This may yield the advantage of providing more control in regards to achieving the desired
emotional outcome. Furthermore, one ought to be aware that there is a discernible imbalance present in
the data-sets with male participants dominating the experiments in comparison to female participants;
WESAD (12 males and 3 females), K-EmoCon (20 males and 12 females), as well as AMIGOS (27 males and
13 females). This may result in concerns regarding the validity of the generalisable properties when
considering female participants and may ultimately contribute towards greater inequality due to the
design of accessible artificial intelligence solutions being for males. However, alongside the need for the
inclusion of females there also appears to be an absence of literature focusing on emotion recognition
with reference to children and elderly, which may add to the already existing discrepancy in regards to
the validity of the feasibility claims due to a lack of research on this, hence the need for a critical evalu-
ation of this. Yet one may argue that the lack of these participants originates from the fact that there
is an absence of data available due to these groups constituting a smaller part of the population when
considering diagnosis of mental health disorders, as well as the strict control and regulations in relation
to the use of data from children within openly available sources. Another shortcoming is perhaps also
the lack of large scale studies as these are rather resource and labour extensive, hence predominantly
consisting of medium-sized studies of less than 40 participants as it is the case in [90, 102, 129].
Algorithms and Physiological Signals:
The field of research has lead itself to the implementation of various artificial intelligence solutions
in the form of static predictive models to assess and evaluate the viability of emotion recognition for
physiological signals from wearable devices.
The primary trend has here been to employ standard
machine learning models such as k-nearest neighbours, naive Bayes, support vector machines, decision
trees, random forests, gradient boosting and adaptive boosting, with the utilisation of these being present
in various sources, see for instance [19, 129, 138, 152]. This naturally leads itself to the application of
feature extraction and thus the requirement for a thorough apprehension of the problem at hand and
the accompanying specifications, in order to establish, distinguish, and select, the features which are
appropriate for the concern of the analysis. We argue that there appears to be no standard within
the literature for pre-processing as well as feature extraction in regards to the physiological signals, as
evident from the fact that for instance none of [19, 129, 138, 152] apply the same set of procedures. In
extension to this, exemplifying that data-set and research specific procedures are vital in enabling the
accomplishment of adequate performance. The sources exploit different physiological signals, with [138]
using the physiological signal of heart-rate, [19] using the electrocardiogram, while [152] independently
uses the electrocardiogram, the electromyography, the electroencephalography, the electrodermal activity,
the blood-volume-pulse, skin temperature as well as the Piezo-electric repiration. Likewise, the emotional
states of interest may differ depending on the objectives of the study. The studies of [138, 129, 152] all
include a neutral/baseline state to correct for participant-dependent characteristics.
Here [138] uses the emotional states of happy and sad, and achieve adequate discrimination between
the three emotional states (neutral, happy and sad) for most of the static predictive models with the
best outcomes in regards to the accuracy being; 62.7% (k-nearest neighbour), 84% (decision tree), 73.3%
(random forest), 84% (gradient boosting), and 70.7% (adaptive boosting). The paper also explores the
setting of pairwise classification (confer [138] for further details). The paper conclusively recommends
the boosting algorithms.
The study present in [19] uses the emotional states of amusement and sadness in order to discrim-
inate between negative and positive levels of valence. The static predictive models acquire adequate
performances with accuracies including; 58.56% (k-nearest neighbour) 61.38% (decision tree), 59.17%
(random forest), 61.07% (gradient boosting), and 73.52% (support vector machine).
The paper of [152] uses different emotional states, comprising of negative valence and low arousal,
negative valence and high arousal, positive valence and low arousal as well as positive valence and high
arousal. However, note that the different emotional states are applied at different settings within the
experiment (confer [152] for details). The exploration of this paper consists of emotion recognition
under three different scenarios; training-set and test-set with no physical activity, training-set with no
physical activity and test-set with participants walking on stairs as well as both training-set and test-set
==PAGE:14==
2 Literature Review
5
with participants walking on stairs. It was here found that best static predictive model for the first
scenario was the naive Bayes using the physiological signal of skin temperature, yielding an accuracy
of 86.86%. The second scenario did not yield satisfactory results, thus demonstrating that; a model
based upon well-behaved data with no physical activity does not generalise adequately to the real-life
setting of walking on stairs. For the third scenario the best static predictive model was the decision
tree using the skin temperature resulting in the accuracy of 91.25%. Hereby emphasising the necessity
for collecting both the training-set and the test-set under the same conditions.
The variation in the results of the static predictive models may stem from different reasons, although
the primary one perhaps being the differences in data-sets. Moreover, the classification task, the pre-
processing, the feature extraction and the hyper-parameter settings may likewise also add to the already
existing discrepancy in performances evident in the literature. Moreover, due to the insu’¼Ćicient amounts
of data, arising from aforesaid reasons, most scholars such as [90, 129, 138], choose to implement leave-
one-participant-out cross-validation in order to perform any compulsory evaluation of the reliability and
generalisable properties of the static predictive models.
Despite most scholars considering standard, simple to implement machine learning models, some
scholars have also appealed to more complex deep-learning models such as for instance convolutional
neural-networks.
Here [128] proposes three different architectures to discriminate between low and
high levels of arousal based upon the physiological signal of electrodermal activity. The three different
architectures being; a pure convolutional neural-network, a combination of a convolutional and long
short-term memory neural-network as well as a convolutional and bidirectional long short-term memory
neural-network hybrid, yielding good performances with F1-scores of 81.95%, 88.95% and 91.02% respec-
tively, they further compare these with various pre-trained models. Consequently avoiding the need for
explicit feature extraction due to the property of representational learning and thus circumventing the
intrinsic problems associated to this.
Mental Health Disorders:
The use of emotion recognition for physiological signals from wearable devices to address artificial
intelligence solutions within the psychiatry constitutes an active research field, which is constantly
evolving and providing new directions and literature of relevance. Yet, we are not aware of any current
use of emotion recognition in this setting for obsessive-compulsive-disorder, albeit its use in research
for other mental health disorders such as for instance autism-spectrum-disorder, where [112] proposes
a system for analysing individuals with autism-spectrum-disorder, enabling caretakers to recognise the
current emotional state of the individual in order to subside certain behavioural issues, using phys-
iological signals from wearable devices with a support vector machine. It has also been applied for
bipolar-disorder, where [147] for instance proposes the development of an emotion recognition system
to distinguish between different emotional states associated with bipolar-disorder in order to serve as a
viable decision support system for assisting the assessment of emotional states within patient care. Here
likewise using physiological signals from wearable devices and applying various machine learning models.
Other mental health disorders includes, but is not limited to, for instance anxiety-disorders [148] and
schizophrenia [22]. Thus constituting a significant gap in the literature, which ought to be of immense
interest to explore and address in further details, hence the reason for investigating the prospects of im-
plementing similar approaches for obsessive-compulsive-disorder and assess the feasibility in the real-life
setting of physiological signals from wearable devices.
An important aspect in relation to employing artificial intelligence solution within the psychiatry is
the eminent problem of advancing from ambulatory settings to real-life. Due to the variations between
participants as well as the presence of noise amplifying in real-life, induce several intricacies in perform-
ing emotion recognition outside the constraint of ambulatory settings [126, 152]. This is in particular
an issue for the physiological signal of the blood-volume-pulse which is especially prone to noise [12,
84], hence limiting its applicability in real-life settings. Various signal-processing approaches have been
proposed in the literature for extracting relevant information [123, 152]. However, there appears to be
a lack of prominent research on the influence of noise on feature specific characteristics and as such how
the features perform under the conditions of noise.
==PAGE:15==
2 Literature Review
6
Conclusion:
We have found that one of the primary challenges within the respective field of research, is the fact
that there appears to be few standards within the literature. This is for instance with reference to
pre-processing and feature extraction as papers predominantly rely on their own data-sets yielding
different acquisition devices, different physiological signals and different procedures, which may cause
the research to not transfer well to settings of others [143]. However, there appears to be consensus in
regards to the use of machine learning methods such as models and evaluation techniques. Furthermore,
we have found that there occurs to be a gap within the research in addressing children, elderly, females
and people with obsessive-compulsive-disorder, alongside a lack of large-scale studies as well as research
on the physiological signal of blood-volume-pulse.
==PAGE:16==
CHAPTER 3
Preliminaries
Before investigating the problem of emotion recognition using physiological signals from wearable devices
in further details, we will need to establish the mathematical foundation as well as the most essential
concepts in signal-processing and machine learning necessary to the study of this subject.
We will therefore start by introducing a number of preliminary subjects relating to both general
mathematics as well as more specifically signal-processing and machine learning.
Note that this chapter does not provide an exhaustive description of the subsequent subjects.
Statistical Learning: We start by introducing the concept of statistical learning, which provides the
foundation for the statistics and the machine learning we will utilise throughout the dissertation.
The primary references for this section are the textbooks [10, 54, 92]
Distributions: Probability distributions constitute an essential concept in probability theory as well as
statistics. We shall here present some fundamental probability distributions, which are commonly
used in modelling. In particular the Gaussian distribution, which provides many analytical results
central to the underlying theory of modelling thus making it interesting to investigate its properties
and the results derived from it. This section is based on a variety of references such as [26, 107]
Optimisation: It is customary in statistics to establish models to represent and describe data, hence
the need for optimal model parameters which requires numerical optimisation as complex models
are inherently non-linear. We will here consider the general setting of optimisation and present
some well-established results as well as algorithms concerning unconstrained numerical optimisa-
tion. The primary source of this section will be [48, 96] supported by the articles [68, 78].
Transformations: Mathematical transformations are applied all throughout time series analysis and
signal-processing. We thus present a selection of important transformation and their properties.
The work is mostly based upon the textbooks [15, 21, 86].
Signal-Processing: A thorough apprehension of signal-processing constitutes a necessity for under-
standing the pre-processing of the data, hence the reason for providing an introduction to essential
concepts within signal-processing. Here using various references including [58, 86].
For the sake of simplicity, the content of this chapter (and the dissertation in general) will mostly con-
sist of univariate theory, albeit most elements being generalisable to the corresponding multivariate case.
LetŌĆÖs now start by presenting the field of statistical learning and some its most fundamental results.
==PAGE:17==
3.1 Statistical Learning
8
3.1
Statistical Learning
The field of statistical learning is a fundamental field within both statistics and machine learning, which
concerns itself with models and algorithms for learning from data [54].
There exists different categories within statistical learning, of which we here will inspect the categories
constituting the most vital roles for the statistical learning considered in this dissertation [63].
Supervised Learning refers to the setting where both observations and targets are available, thus
yielding the learning task of inferring the function f based on the data-set.
Unsupervised Learning contrary to supervised learning only concerns observations, hence referring
to the set of techniques for analysing these observations. This includes but is not limited to;
establishing patterns such as in cluster analysis as well as finding lower-dimensional representations
as for instance done by an autoencoder (AE) [48].
We shall in this chapter primarily consider the setting of supervised learning, although we shall also
briefly discuss a simple unsupervised learning algorithm for dimensionality reduction.
3.1.1
Supervised Learning
Supervised learning covers the statistical inference problem of estimating a function from a data-set [63].
In the following, we consider a data-set of n observations xi ŌłłX , here X = Rp with each dimension
referred to as a feature [56], and corresponding targets yi ŌłłY (possibly multi-dimensional). A data-set
thus comprise n ŌłłN examples; D = {(x1, y1), . . . , (xn, yn)}. Further, note that one often includes the
additional assumption of the examples being independent and identically distributed (iid) [48].
There is considered to be two types of tasks in supervised learning; regression and classification.
The task depends upon the data, more specifically the targets. In case of the target for instance being
continuous and real-valued id est yi ŌłłR it is a regression task, while in the case of the target being
categorical id est yi Ōłł{1, . . . , C} (C ŌłłNŌēź2 is the number of classes) it is a classification task [92].
A practical consideration in supervised learning is to split the data-set into three subsets, referred
to as the training-set, the validation-set and the test-set. The purpose of this is to ensure a proper
evaluation of the generalisable properties of the model. The training-set is solely used for finding the
model parameters with the use of optimisation. Successive to this the model makes predictions on the
validation-set to provide an unbiased evaluation on the training-set as well as to perform model selection
and hyper-parameter optimisation by making an assessment of the fitting [92]. This is also known as
the hold-out method [56]. Once we pick the best model we may train it again using all available data id
est both the training-set and the validation-set, according to [92]. The test-set then yields an unbiased
evaluation of the final model by testing on data which has not been used during training, in order to
assess how well the model generalises to new data [10]. There are multiple approaches for dividing the
data-set, although a standard approach is simply to divide the data-set into a training-set and a test-set
using a 80/20 ratio and then further splitting the training-set into a training-set and validation-set with
a 75/25 ratio. Note that the subsets ought to follow the same probability distribution and that the
splits ought to not be critical, hence in the case of independent and identically distributed examples
one may permute the data-set prior to splitting [66, 131].
Supervised learning refers to the set of methods and approaches for estimating the function f [63].
The objective is therefore to find a model with reference to a sensible procedure in order to acquire a
suitable model that generalises to new data. This is done by achieving an adequate fit of the model by
avoiding overfitting, which refers to a model which is for instance too complex compared to what can be
justified by the data, hence fitting the training-set too well leading to poor performance (generalisation)
for the test-set. Likewise, avoiding underfitting which contrary to overfitting refers to a model which is
too simple to represent the underlying structure of the data, hence having a poor fit to the training-set
and as a consequence having unsatisfactory performance [48]. A good fit thus ought to provide an
adequate compromise between these two settings. We shall return to this issue later.
==PAGE:18==
3.1 Statistical Learning

LetŌĆÖs now present a more formal description of the problem of supervised learning. Consider the
space of possible observations X and the space of possible targets Y . Further assume that the data
originates from an underlying unknown probability distribution p(x, y) and that there exists an unknown
function f : X ŌåÆY describing the relationship between the observations and corresponding target [54]

Notice that additional specifications will be provided when necessary.
The supervised learning problem now consists of estimating the function f. Let H be an appropriate
space of functions, which is known as the hypothesis-space (the space of functions under considerations).
Furthermore, let L : Y ├Ś Y ŌåÆRŌēź0 be a loss-function, which describes and measures the difference in
predictions and targets, the objective is therefore to find the estimate of the function f which minimises
the expectation of the loss-function. Note that the loss-function ought to be specified according to the
problem in question. The expected loss-function (also referred to as the expected risk) is [48]
Ep(x,y)[L(f(x), y)].
(3.2)
Note that the only varying quantity of the loss-function and thus also the expected risk is indeed f.

(3.24)
We have here been able to decompose the expression into a sum consisting of the (expectation of the)
squared bias term, the (expectation of the) variance term and an intrinsic noise term [10]. The objective
is now to find a model, which yields a desirable compromise between bias and variance. Here flexible
models will have a low bias and high variance, whereas simpler models will tend to have high bias and
low variance [10]. This is known as the problem of the bias-variance trade-off.
One approach for finding a model, which yields a su’¼Ćicient compromise between bias and variance
is to use cross-validation (CV), which we will subsequently introduce. Model selection is done by finding
the model which produces the best estimate of expected risk and then selecting this model. In order
to estimate the expected risk we may utilise a validation-set, although this may not feasible in the case
of limited amounts of data, as the we will then not have enough data to complete the training of the
model and produce a reliable estimate of future performance [92], hence why we mostly implement this
approach in the field of deep learning (see section C.4) where data is abundant and models are too
complex to utilise cross-validation due to the computational complexity associated, as it shortly will
become evident. Another simple approach is therefore to instead use the cross-validation. In cross-
validation we split the training-set into K ŌłłNŌēź2 folds, then for each fold k Ōłł{1, . . . , K} we train on
all remaining K ŌłÆ1 folds, and subsequently use the kŌĆÖth fold for validation. We then compute the
average error across the folds yielding an estimate of the expected risk and the model selection can
then be done based upon this. This is known as K-fold cross-validation [92]. Note for K = n, that
we acquire the so-called leave-one-out cross-validation. This may be desirable in settings where the
amount of available data constitutes a limitation [56]. However, this may result in high variance due
to folds being too similar. Likewise, also increasing the number of models which we have to fit, hence
K = 10 fold is often more appropriate [54]. We are able to generalise this to other settings such as for
instance leave-one-participant-out cross-validation. However, the approach of cross-validation provides
no measures of uncertainty. A standard approach is simply to use the standard error of the mean id est
╦åŽā/ŌłÜn [92], and a common heuristic is then to pick the simplest model which is no more than a single
standard error of the mean above the estimate of the expected risk for the best model, this is referred to
as the one standard error rule [92]. This often picks a model with similar performance in regards to the
estimate of the expected risk although a model which is more regularised thus compensating for cross-
validation otherwise choosing too complex of a model [54]. Note that the respective cross-validation
schemes assume that the examples are independent and identically distributed [48, 131]. Additional
details on cross-validation are available in [54, 131].
Another important concept within statistical inference is the problem of multiple hypothesis testing.
Consider the setting of a hypothesis test using a significance level ╬▒, then the probability of falsely
rejection the null-hypothesis id est making a type I error is ╬▒ [16]. However, consider now the setting of
m ŌłłN hypotheses tests with the null-hypotheses H0,1, . . . , H0,m and corresponding p-values p1, . . . , pm.
Assume that that the hypotheses tests are independent and m0 ŌŖåNŌēżm is the set of true hypotheses.

or equal to ╬▒ by rejecting null-hypothesis i if pi Ōēż╬▒/m [54]. To see why this is the case, consider

3.1.2
Principal Component Analysis
We continue the exposition by considering the unsupervised learning algorithm of principal component
analysis (PCA) for finding lower-dimensional representations of data-sets solely consisting of observations.
Principal component analysis constitutes of finding an orthonormal basis comprising of the eigenvectors
of the covariance matrix ordered by the size of the corresponding eigenvalues. These are referred to
as principal components [137]. Principal component analysis is thus a linear orthogonal transformation
such that the features in the new coordinate system are mutually uncorrelated and ordered by variance.
Note that it is vital to standardise the data-set prior to performing principal component analysis when
features have different scales, as features with high variance will otherwise be biased and dominate the
first principal components [131]. Assume that the data-set is standardised and consider an observation
matrix X ŌłłRn├Śp, where each row corresponds to an observation and each column to a feature.
The objective is now to find an orthonormal matrix P Ōłł[ŌłÆ1, 1]p├Śp for making a linear orthogonal
transformation of the observations Y = XP , such that the new covariance matrix ╬ŻY =
1
nY T Y
(we may replace this by its unbiased counterpart) is a diagonal matrix, which means that the features
in the new coordinate system are mutually uncorrelated. We shall subsequently see that P consists
of columns with the principal components of X, id est the ordered eigenvectors of the covariance
(correlation) matrix KX = 1
nXT X [137].
Note that a covariance matrix is indeed symmetric. Recall that a matrix A ŌłłRm├Śm is symmetric
if A = AT [142]. Since any matrix A ŌłłRm├Św has the property that
(AAT )T = (AT )T AT = AAT ,
(3.28)
(AT A)T = AT (AT )T = AT A.
(3.29)
Here using the fact that for any two matrices A ŌłłRm├Św and B ŌłłRm├Św, it holds that [89]
(AB)T = BT AT
and
(AT )T = A.

Before proceeding further, we provide two supplementary results from linear algebra.
The first is that; a matrix A ŌłłRm├Śm is symmetric if and only if it is orthogonally diagonalisable,
meaning there exists an invertible matrix F ŌłłRm├Śm and a diagonal matrix Em├Śm such that [89, 137]
A = F EF ŌłÆ1 ŌćöE = F ŌłÆ1AF .
(3.32)
The matrix F here diagonalises the matrix A [89, 137].
the second is that; a symmetric matrix is diagonalisable by a matrix of its orthonormal eigenvectors.
Thus asserting that the columns of F consist of the eigenvectors of A, while the diagonal of E consists
of the corresponding eigenvalues, and that the relevant matrices indeed exist [31, 137].
Note for any orthogonal matrix A ŌłłRm├Św, that [137]
AT A = I ŌćöAŌłÆ1 = AT .
(3.33)
Returning to the original problem of finding the matrix P . By selecting each column of P to be the
eigenvectors (ordered according to the size of the corresponding eigenvalues) of the covariance matrix
KX and using the previous results we are able to rewrite the covariance matrix KY , as follows
KY = P KXP = P T (P EP ŌłÆ1)P = P ŌłÆ1P EP ŌłÆ1P = E.
(3.34)
It is hereby evident that we obtain a diagonalisation of the covariance matrix KY such that the
features are mutually uncorrelated and the axes are ordered by variance, as desired. Therefore, in order
to perform dimensionality reduction using principal component analysis, we only retain a subset of
principal components when doing the linear orthogonal transformation [137].
Recall that a covariance matrix is positive semi-definite [114], hence the eigenvalues are non-negative.
The reader is referred to [137] for more information and detail on principal component analysis.
3.2
Distributions
Probability distributions constitute a vital role within probability theory and statistics, hence also
the likes of both time series analysis and machine learning. Hereby providing the reason for being a
quintessential topic of interest of which study is necessary, in order to establish and avail appropriate
analysis and modelling. We will here present and discuss the most essential probability distributions,
which will be considered throughout this dissertation.
However, prior to presenting the different probability distributions of interest, letŌĆÖs start by introducing
the concept of entropy. The entropy of a stochastic variable is a non-negative measure expressing the
information available id est the amount of uncertainty present, in relation to its distribution [10, 116].
The entropy of a stochastic variable X with respect to a probability distribution p(x) is [10, 48, 136]
Q(X) =

is the notion of relative entropy or Kullback-Leibler divergence, which is of relevance in statistics and
machine learning, for instance in Bayesian statistics where it is utilised in the variational inference

relates to the field of information theory (for additional information and details please see [48, 72, 136]).
Remark. The concept of entropy was proposed by Claude Shannon, in his original work from 1948: ŌĆØA
Mathematical Theory of CommunicationŌĆØ, hence why it is sometimes referred to as Shannon entropy.
The relative entropy or Kullback-Leibler divergence was originally proposed by Solomon Kullback and
Richard Leibler in their article from 1951: ŌĆØOn Information and Su’¼ĆiciencyŌĆØ.
We will now proceed by introducing the different probability distributions of interest.
3.2.1
Bernoulli Distribution
The Bernoulli distribution is a binary probability distribution for a stochastic variable with either an
outcome of 1 with probability p Ōłł[0, 1] or an outcome of 0 with probability 1 ŌłÆp. The Bernoulli dis-
tribution hereby gives rise to the modelling of events such as (biased) coin-flips, true/false-statements,
yes/no-question, success/failure, et cetera. The Bernoulli distribution is a discrete probability distribu-
tion and as such it invokes a probability mass function, which is given as follows [10]
q(x|p) = px(1 ŌłÆp)1ŌłÆx for x Ōłł{0, 1}.
(3.37)
The Bernoulli distribution has mean p and variance p(1 ŌłÆp) and it uses the notation Bernoulli(p).
Remark. The Bernoulli distribution takes its name after the Swiss mathematician Jacob Bernoulli.
3.2.2
Uniform Distribution
The uniform distribution appears both as a discrete and a continuous probability distribution and as
the name suggests it is a probability distribution for a stochastic variable where either each outcome
(discrete case) or each interval with the same length on the support (continuous case) is equally probable.
Starting with the discrete uniform distribution, then n outcomes each have probability 1/n and it can be
used to model for instance the outcome of a fair die. Furthermore, note that the Bernoulli distribution
with p = 1/2 is a special case, where the only two outcomes are 0 and 1. The discrete uniform probability
distribution is non-parametric, but it can be represented by considering all integers between two integer
outcomes for instance a and b. The mean is then (a + b)/2 and the variance is

(b ŌłÆa + 1)2 ŌłÆ1

3.2.5
Exponential Distribution
The exponential distribution is a continuous probability distribution for real-valued stochastic variables.
It often models the time between occurrences of event, hence being a sensible modelling choice in many
situations [65]. The probability density function of the distribution is given as follows [65, 107]
p(x|╬╗) = ╬╗eŌłÆx╬╗ for x ŌłłRŌēź0,
(3.69)
where ╬╗ ŌłłR>0 is a rate parameter. Note that the exponential probability distribution likewise can be
specified by its scale parameter (the inverse of the rate parameter). It has mean 1/╬╗ and variance 1/╬╗2.

3.2.6
Poisson Distribution
Lastly, we will present the Poisson distribution, which is a discrete probability distribution expressing
the probability of a given number of events occurring within a fixed interval if these events occur
independent, singly and with a constant rate [107]. The Poisson distribution is in many settings applied
for modelling purposes [107]. The probability mass function of the Poisson distribution is [107]
p(x|╬╗) = ╬╗xeŌłÆ╬╗

of independent Poisson variables will indeed yield itself to a new Poisson variable with rate parameter
specified by the sum of the other rate parameters. The Poisson distributions uses the notation Pois(╬╗).
Remark. The Poisson distribution is named after the French mathematician Sim├®on Denis Poisson.
3.3
Optimisation
In most applications it is not possible to acquire an analytical closed-form solution to an optimisation
problem as these are predominantly non-linear, hence the need for numerical optimisation algorithms.
We shall here briefly discuss the most simple non-linear unconstrained optimisation algorithm, namely
gradient descent. LetŌĆÖs give a short, although concise, introduction to the discipline of optimisation.
Optimisation concerns the findings of extrema points. Given an objective function J : X ŌåÆY we are
interested in finding the point wŌłŚŌłłX where the function attains its extrema either local or global
minima/maxima (most algorithm are attentive to the minima). Recall that a maximisation problem
can be transformed into a minimisation problem and vice versa, simply by a change of sign of the
objective function. For an objective function which is continuously differentiable to an adequate degree,
a su’¼Ćicient condition for a local minima wŌłŚ(also global in the convex case) is the following [96]
ŌłćJ(wŌłŚ) = 0 and Ōłć2J(wŌłŚ) Ō¬░0.
(3.71)
Stating that the gradient at the point wŌłŚought to be 0 also known as a stationary point, while the
Hessian at the point wŌłŚshould also be positive semi-definite (have non-negative eigenvalues), indicating
that no local change will yield a better or worse result in regards to finding the minima. Moving away
will thus make the gradient change in an upwards direction leading to an increase in the function value.
LetŌĆÖs now describe the simple gradient descent algorithm for finding the minima of an objective function.
This can for instance be used to optimise a function over a set of parameters exempli gratia finding the
most optimal weights for a non-linear regression in regards to minimising the sum of squared errors,
hence such numerical optimisation algorithm proving their usefulness in areas such as statistics, machine
learning, et cetera. Note in statistics and machine learning that the objective function depends on a
given data-set D, although we shall for the moment not express this dependency explicitly.
3.3.1
Gradient Descent
The gradient descent algorithm is part of a broader class of numerical optimisation algorithms known
as line-search methods, which employ the following framework; assume we are at a point wi ŌłłRD, then
the next point can be found iteratively by moving along a search direction pi specified by the algorithm
wi+1 = wi + ╬Ępi for i ŌłłN0,
(3.72)
where ╬Ę ŌłłR>0 is the step-size (or learning-rate in machine learning). Note that the step-size ╬Ę sometimes
depends on i and as a consequence can change within each iteration. In the particular case of the gradient
descent algorithm the search direction is given by the negative gradient
pi = ŌłÆŌłćJ(wi),
(3.73)
==PAGE:30==
3.3 Optimisation
21
the idea of the gradient descent algorithm is that moving in the opposite direction of the gradient at a
point w will lead towards the minima most e’¼Ćiciently due to this being the direction of steepest descent,
hence attempting to exploit the (naive) intuition that the direction of steepest descent will in fact lead
to the most e’¼Ćicient descent (although this is not necessarily the case). In order to see why this is the
direction of steepest descent, consider the following first-order Taylor expansion [96]
J(wi + ╬Ępi) ŌēłJ(wi) + ╬ĘpT
i ŌłćJ(wi).
(3.74)
Evidently the rate of change in the function at wi along the direction of pi is simply the coe’¼Ćicient for
the step-size ╬Ę id est pT
i ŌłćJ(wi). If we (without loss of generality) constrain the search direction to be
the unit direction then the steepest descent is the solution to the following optimisation problem [96]
inf
piŌłłRD pT
i ŌłćJ(wi) s.t ŌłźpiŌłź= 1.
(3.75)
However, since pT
i ŌłćJ(wi) = ŌłźpiŌłźŌłźŌłćJ(wi)Ōłźcos(v) = ŌłźŌłćJ(wi)Ōłźcos(v), where v is the angle between
the search direction and the gradient, then the minima is clearly obtained by cos(v) = ŌłÆ1, hence
pi = ŌłÆŌłćJ(wi)
ŌłźŌłćJ(wi)Ōłź.
(3.76)
This is exactly the negative gradient direction, although here scaled due to the imposed constraint of a
unit direction. To summarise; the gradient descent algorithm, provided an initial guess of the minima
w0, creates a sequence of points until the monotonically decreasing sequence consisting of the function
evaluated at these points converges to a minima (specified by some convergence criteria) or reaches an
upper bound on the number of iterations allowed.
This is the gradient descent algorithm, which is a part of the more general class of numerical
optimisation algorithms known as line-search methods, where gradient descent is the simplest version,
Figure 3.2: Gradient Descent with ╬Ę = 0.05 for the six-hump camel-back function.
other algorithms do not necessarily solely depend on the gradient but rather more complex search
directions for instance NewtonŌĆÖs algorithm, which also makes use of the second derivative. Notice, that
the gradient descent algorithm is rather simple and correspondingly it has the advantage that it does
not require the second derivative nor any evaluations of this, although as a consequence it can have
slow convergence and may not be as e’¼Ćicient for more complicated problems as other algorithms [96].
Note that the purpose of the step-size is vital in regards to the success of the algorithm and there-
fore how the step-size is chosen is of utmost importance. This is due to a small step-size yielding slow
convergence, if the upper bound on the number of iterations is not exceeded, while a too large step-size
will inevitably lead to divergence. Choosing the step-size is therefore an important practical problem.
==PAGE:31==
3.3 Optimisation
22
There is multiple approaches to this problem, the reader is referred to [96] for classical approaches,
although we shall here only discuss the approach of using an adaptive step-size.
Algorithm 1 Gradient Descent Algorithm.
1: Given an initial guess w0 ŌłłRD.
2: Pick a step-size ╬Ę ŌłłR>0.
3: Set i = 0.
4: repeat
5:
Compute direction pi = ŌłÆŌłćJ(wi).
6:
Compute next iterate wi+1 = wi + ╬Ępi.
7: until Convergence or i > imax.
Now, before discussing the specifics of an adaptive step-size, we will introduce a slight, nonetheless
very popular, variation of the gradient descent algorithm.
3.3.2
Stochastic Gradient Descent
We will now introduce a variation of the previously presented gradient descent algorithm, namely
stochastic gradient descent (SGD). This variation, as the name suggests, can be regarded as a stochastic
approximation to the gradient descent algorithm. By replacing the gradient by an estimate computed
from a randomly sampled subset of the data-set, hence acquiring a noisy estimate. Hereby reducing
the computational task and thus ought to perform faster and more e’¼Ćicient in regards to both time and
memory complexity, especially in higher dimensions.
Consider now the following optimisation problem [64, 78]
inf
wŌłłRD J(w, D) where J(w, D) = 1

J (w, Ds) .
(3.77)
Note that D = {Ds}S
s=1 denotes the entire data-set, while Ds denotes the corresponding sŌĆÖth subset,
which is referred to as a (mini)batch. We here assume that each (mini)batch has the same cardinality
and that n is divisible by S. Let ╦£s be a uniform sample from the sequence {1, . . . , S} such that
ŌłćwJ(w, D) = E╦£s

(3.78)
We can hereby approximate the gradient by sampling a random subset of the data-set.

An extension to the stochastic gradient descent algorithm can be obtained by appealing to the
method of momentum in order to accelerate the procedure and attain faster convergence. The method
of momentum consist of an exponential smoothing of the gradients. The method introduces a variable
v for the velocity (direction and speed) of the current point, defined accordingly [48]
pi = ŌłÆŌłćwJ(wi, D╦£si),
(3.80)
vi = ╬▒viŌłÆ1 + ╬Ępi,
(3.81)
where ╬▒ Ōłł[0, 1[ is a hyper-parameter indicating the rate at which the contributions of previous gradients
decay, which in time series analysis is referred to as a smoothing factor [86]. The next point is then
wi+1 = wi + vi.
(3.82)
The size of the step thus depends on the magnitude as well as the alignment of a sequence of gradients.
Supplementary variations of the method of momentum exists, which can be accessed at [48, 120].
3.3.3
Adaptive Step-Size
In practice we are not interested in using too small of a step-size due to this influencing the performance
of the algorithms while we neither want to use too large of a step-size as the algorithms will then
diverge, hence we often tend to use an adaptive step-size scheme. Especially for stochastic optimisation
algorithms, adaptive step-size schemes ought to be sensible due to the issues relating to the convergence
of such algorithms. A simple approach would be to use a step-size schedule by for instance starting
with a large step-size and then gradually making it smaller as a function of the iterations. We will now
present an adaptive step-size scheme known as adaptive moment estimation (Adam) proposed in [68],
albeit multiple adaptive step-size schemes being available. This scheme consists of features similar to
the method of momentum. Here we first introduce the first and (raw) second moments of the gradients

3.3.4
Regularisation
The supervised learning problem of inferring an unknown function with respect to a finite and often
noisy data-set is inherently an ill-posed problem, multiple solutions are indeed applicable. It is therefore
customary to impose additional constraints in the optimisation procedure in order to ensure the acqui-
sition of a simpler set of model parameter estimates, hence preventing overfitting and thus achieving
better generalisable properties of the resulting model. There exists multiple regularisation schemes,
although the general regularisation scheme is predominantly of the following form [54, 92, 116]
inf
fŌłłH
1
n
n
X
i=1
L(f(xi), yi) + ╬╗Q{f},
(3.93)
where H is an appropriate space of functions, Q : H ŌåÆRŌēź0 is a penalty functional and ╬╗ ŌłłRŌēź0 controls
the weight given to the penalty term (confer [71] for more information of functional analysis). We often
consider penalty functionals in the form of norms, as to penalise set of parameter estimates whose
corresponding norm yields a large magnitude. We shall see later that we can also exploit regularisation
for feature importance. Further details regarding the specifics will be evident in forthcoming chapters.
3.4
Transformations
In time series analysis and signal-processing it is common practice to employ the use of mathematical
transformations, such as to for instance convert from one domain to another. We will here present some
vital mathematical transformations, which will be applied and utilised throughout this dissertation.
LetŌĆÖs start by considering the most important as well as the most popular mathematical transformation.

We are now ready to introduce the Fourier transformation. The Fourier transformation is an important

Definition 3.4.3 (Fourier Transformation). The Fourier transformation associates to each function

Remark. The Fourier transformation is named in honour of the French mathematician and physicist,

3.4.2
Laplace Transformation
Another commonly applied mathematical transformation in time series analysis, signal-processing as
well as the analysis of differential equations is the Laplace transformation. The Laplace transformation
converts a function of the real spatial or temporal domain into a function of the complex spatial or
temporal frequency domain (known as the s-domain) [2]. The definition now follows [2, 86].
Definition 3.4.4 (Laplace Transformation). For a su’¼Ćiciently well-behaved function l : RŌēź0 ŌåÆC the

This is the unilateral case and it can be extended to the bilateral case by considering R. Furthermore,
note that the Laplace transformation is linear, which follows immediately from its definition [86].
Remark. The Laplace transformation takes its name from the French Polymath Pierre-Simon Laplace.

3.5
Signal-Processing
The theory of signal-processing relates, as the name suggests, to the processing of signals, meaning
systems converting input signals to output signals. The field of signal-processing is indispensable when
working with signals, and is for instance applied when filtering a signal, hence being an absolute neces-
sity for the understanding of the remaining of this dissertation. Thus the need for a brief and concise
description of the classical field of signal-processing. We will here present some fundamental topics and
results, which shall be applied throughout the remaining of this dissertation.
We shall start by considering and describing the basic characteristics of linear systems [21, 86].
3.5.1
Linear Systems
A signal is either continuous (analog signal) or discrete (digital signal). The prior being a function often
of time {y(t), t ŌłłR} and the latter being a sequence {yt, t ŌłłZ} (we may also use subsets of these).
However, note that a discrete signal in practice is the result of sampling a continuous signal [86].
The field of signal-processing concerns itself with systems converting an input signal to an output
signal. A system is therefore given by a mathematical operator F often known as a filter [21, 86].
We will here consider the simplest type of system available, which is a linear system [17, 21].
Definition 3.5.1 (Linear System). Consider x1, x2 : R ŌåÆC and a, b ŌłłC. A system is then linear if
F[ax1 + bx2](t) = aF[x1](t) + bF[x2](t).
(3.123)
A simplification of a linear system is possible by considering a linear time-invariant system (LTI)
which attains an additional property given by the following definition [17, 21, 86].
Definition 3.5.2 (Time-Invariant System). Consider x : R ŌåÆC and Žä ŌłłR. A system satisfying
FTŽä[x](t) = TŽäF[x](t),
(3.124)
is then said to be time-invariant, where TŽä[x](t) = x(t ŌłÆŽä) is the translation or shift operator.
Note; for the discrete-case this is often referred to as a shift-invariant system (LSI) [86]. The analysis
of signals is done in both time domain as well as frequency domain. For linear time-invariant systems
in the time domain, we can express the relation between the input {xt} and the output {yt} of the
system by an impulse response function, according to the following convolutions [86]

3.5.2
Sampling
In a practical setting we only evaluate a continuous signal at a finite number of discrete time instances,
we refer to this procedure as sampling a signal. The sampling of a signal leads to a loss of information,
which we shall examine in further details.
To obtain the sampling of a continuous signal x(t) at

We hereby observe that sampling a signal in the frequency domain yields a periodic function. Fur-
thermore, it is seen that; if ╦£x(Žē) contains values outside the interval of [ŌłÆ1/2T, 1/2T], then these are
indistinguishable of those from inside the interval. This is referred to as aliasing [86]. Values of ╦£x(Žē)
from outside the interval will thus be added to those of inside the interval. The frequency Žē = 1/2T
is known as the Nyquist frequency [86], and aliasing occurs if ╦£x(Žē) contains values at frequencies above
the Nyquist frequency. From this it becomes evident that the problem of selecting a sample-period T,
is of utmost importance as the sample-rate ought to be twice as large as the limiting frequency of ╦£x(Žē),
in order to ensure that the limiting frequency does not exceed the Nyquist frequency [86]. This can be
seen from the following; let x(t) be a continuous signal and let ╦£x(Žē) = 0 for |Žē| > Žēl, then
1
T > 2wl Ōćö1
2T > wl.
(3.165)
This leads to the following result [86].
Theorem 3.5.3 (ShannonŌĆÖs Theorem). We may reproduce any continuous signal x(t), by the corre-
sponding sample-signal xs(t), if and only if, it does not contain frequencies above the Nyquist frequency.
Remark. The Nyquist frequency honours the Swedish-American physicist and engineer Harry Nyquist,
while ShannonŌĆÖs theorem honours the American mathematician and engineer Claude Elwood Shannon.
We will now proceed by describing filters which attenuate specific ranges of frequencies. There are
four basic filter types, which are; low-pass (attenuates signals with a frequency higher than the cut-off),
high-pass, bandpass (attenuates signals with a frequency outside the lower and upper cut-offs) and
bandstop. Note here that high-pass is the inverse of low-pass and similar for bandpass and bandstop.
We will now proceed by introducing a type of filter known as a finite impulse response filter.
3.5.3
Infinite Impulse Response Filter
An infinite impulse response filter (IIR) is a filter where the output depends on both the previous inputs
and outputs. The difference equation as well as the transfer function now follows [58]
yt =
q
X
k=0
bkxtŌłÆk ŌłÆ
p
X
k=1
akytŌłÆk,
(3.166)

Pq

(3.167)
where z ŌłłC \ {0} and ak ŌłłC such that 1 + Pp
k=1 akzŌłÆk ╠Ė= 0.
A recursive filter involves feedback, which means that the current output depends on previous
outputs as well as inputs. In most cases the impulse response function of a recursive filter can therefore
theoretically continue indefinitely, hence why it is known as an infinite impulse response filter [58].
A common design method for infinite impulse response filters is to design a continuous-time filter
and then convert it into a discrete-time filter. There is various classes of low-pass continuous-time
filters with a popular class being the Butterworth filter, which is characterised by being monotonic
at all frequencies id est having no local minima nor maxima [58]. A Butterworth filter with a cut-off
frequency of Žēc is according to [61] specified by the following transfer function
H(s) = (ŌłÆ1)p Qp

3.5.4
Bilinear Transformation
We shall here briefly introduce an important mathematical transformation within signal-processing,
which is the bilinear transformation. The bilinear transformation changes a continuous-time system
representation into its discrete-time counterpart and vice versa. Hereby converting the transfer function
of a linear time-invariant system in continuous-time into the corresponding transfer function of a linear
time-invariant system in discrete-time [111]. The bilinear transformation thus constitutes a mapping
of the z-domain to the s-domain and conversely. For a sample-period T ŌłłR>0, we define the bilinear
transformation from the z-domain to the s-domain by the following expression [4]
z = 1 + sT
2

2
for sT ŌłłC \ {2}.
(3.173)
We likewise define the bilinear transformation from the s-domain into the z-domain, accordingly [4]
s = 2

z ŌłÆ1

= 2
T
1 ŌłÆzŌłÆ1
1 + zŌłÆ1

for z ŌłłC \ {ŌłÆ1, 0}.
(3.174)
A central property of the bilinear transformation is that it preserves stability [4]. Recall that; a system in
the z-domain is stable if all poles of the system are within the unit-circle in the z-domain, while a system
in the s-domain is stable if all poles lie in the open left-half of the s-domain [4, 86]. Furthermore, note
that the bilinear transformation introduces a non-linear mapping between the discrete time-frequency
and the continuous-time frequency and vice versa, given by the following expressions [94].
Žēc = 2

as frequency warping and to overcome this it is common practice to apply a pre-warping by using either
equation 3.175 or equation 3.176 before applying the bilinear transformation [58].
The reader is referred to [4] for further details regarding the bilinear transformation.
==PAGE:45==
CHAPTER 4
Data
We will here provide an initial description of the data, which we shall utilise in forthcoming analyses.
We will likewise present the measurement device and the experimental setup for acquiring the data.
4.1
Empatica E4
The Empatica E4 is a commercially available wearable device utilised in the acquisition of physiological
signals and acceleration. It has four sensors measuring and deriving the following signals [144].
The Photoplethysmography (PPG) Sensor measures the blood-volume-pulse (BVP) with a sample-
rate of 64Hz and is provided without a unit due to being derived from two different measures
of reflected light (green and red light respectively). The blood-volume-pulse is used within the
Empatica E4 to derive both the inter-beat-interval (IBI) as well as the heart-rate (HR). The IBI
is computed by detecting peaks in the form of local minimas known as diastolic points and then
computing the interval (in seconds) between adjacent points. It uses an in-built algorithm for
removing incorrect peaks emanating from noise such as for instance motion artifacts. The HR
is measured in beats-per-minute (bmp) with a sample-rate of 1Hz computed from a 10-second
average, where the instantaneous heart-rate can be found by the following
HR = 60
IBI.
(4.1)
The IBI can also be used to estimate additional quantities such as the heart-rate-variability (HRV).
The Galvanic Skin Response (GSR) Sensor evaluates the electrodermal activity (EDA) with a fre-
quency of 4Hz and is provided in the unit microSiemens (┬ĄS). The electrodermal activity refers
to the variations in the electrical properties of the skin. These changes emerge due to the skin
receiving innervating signals from the brain, often on account of experiencing physical activation,
cognitive stimulation, et cetera, thus leading to an increase in the level of sweat and likewise
the electrical conductance of the skin. This is in the Empatica E4 measured by passing a small
current between two electrodes that are in contact with the skin. The complex referred to as
electrodermal activity is conventionally characterised by two components known as the tonic and
the phasic components. The tonic component expresses the slow drift of the baseline, whereas the
phasic component reflects the short-time response to stimuli. The most well-established measures
related to the prior being the skin conductance level (SCL) as well as the skin conductance response
(SCR) respectively. The first being the general conductance of the skin related to general changes
in autonomic arousal, while the second being the rapid fluctuations in the conductance of the skin
often associated with changes in emotional arousal.
The 3-Axis Accelerometer quantifies motion-based activity with reference to the acceleration (ACC)
within the 3-axis, recorded with a sample-rate of 32Hz. It more specifically measures the con-
tinuous gravitational force applied to each of the respective spatial dimensions by units of 1/64g.
Here the configured range is [ŌłÆ2g, 2g] and the data is summarised in a supplied moving-average.
The Infrared Thermopile reads the peripheral skin temperature (TEMP) with a sample-rate of 4Hz
and is measured in the unit degrees Celsius (ŌŚ”C).
==PAGE:46==
4.1 Empatica E4
37
For an overview of the Empatica E4 signals please refer to figure 4.1. Furthermore for a review of
machine learning in the context of signals from wearable devices please consult [3, 12, 125].
Figure 4.1: Illustration of the measured and derived signals of the Empaticia E4.
One should note that; measurements of the blood-volume-pulse often contain noise such as motion
artifacts, hence yielding a low signal-to-noise-ratio (SNR) [84], while measurements of the electrodermal
activity are only valid for recordings longer than 10-15 minutes. This being due to the coupling of
the electrodes, which are in contact with the skin. Furthermore, note that environmental conditions
such as temperature and humidity have significant influence on the acquired recordings, and that the
Empatica E4 has an event tag button allowing for real-time annotations for data labelling purposes [144].
The reader is referred to figure 4.2 for a reference of the signals from the Empatica E4.
Figure 4.2: Examples of the signals recorded during the experiment.
==PAGE:47==
4.2 Experimental Setup
38
4.2
Experimental Setup
The experimental setup for the data acquisition has been developed to reflect and elicit the desired
emotional state of frustration in order to assess the degree to which emotional recognition of frustration
is plausible using physiological signals from the Empatica E4. In the experiment participants are divided
into teams of two, one being an instructor and one being a puzzler. The objective is then to solve as
many Tangram puzzles as possible within the time frame. Here the instructor is the only one with access
to the solutions and the puzzler is the only one admitted to touch the puzzle, hence the instructor has
to communicate to the puzzler, what to assemble and how to assemble it. The team who solve the most
puzzles win. The experimental setup is now as follows; the teams compete in four round, each consisting
of the phases, rest, puzzle, rest. Each round is initialised by a calibration period of one minute.
Succeeding each of the aforementioned phases, the participants are requested to answer a self-report
questionnaire in order to validate that the desired emotional states are indeed elicited. This is done due
to emotions being perceived differently on an individual level, hence the need to represent the subjective
experience as well as to enable the development of personalised models. The self-report questionnaires
consist of the emotions; active, afraid, alert, ashamed, attentive, determined, hostile, inspired, nervous
and upset, rated on 5-point Likert scale. Further, including the addition of frustration and di’¼Ćiculty
evaluated on a 10-point Likert scale, with di’¼Ćiculty only following the puzzle phase.
For an overview of the experimental setup please consult the timeline available in figure 4.3.
Figure 4.3: Timeline for a single round of the experiment.
Note that participants are excluded from the experiment by the following criteria; under the age of
18, use of medication affecting heart-rate, cardio vascular diseases.
4.3
Pilot-Study Data-Set
The completion of a pilot-study has been done prior to the obtainment of clinical data, in order to
enable preliminary studies. The experiment of this pilot-study has been granted an ethical approval by
the institutional review board (IRB) at DTU Compute. The data has been gathered throughout two trials
with 8 and 6 participants respectively, hence a total of 14 participants. Note that similar open-source
data-sets have been considered in the literature. This includes for instance the WESAD data-set [129].
==PAGE:48==
CHAPTER 5
Pre-Processing
We will now presents the cvxEDA algorithm as well as the specifications of the pre-processing procedure.
We utilise the cvxEDA algorithm to perform the decomposition of the physiological signal of EDA, which
will be done in the subsequent pre-processing procedure. The pre-processing procedure will in general
follow standard practices as by the recommendations of [144], with the support of additional literature.
5.1 cvxEDA
The cvxEDA algorithm represents EDA through its tonic component, its phasic component and as well as
an additive noise term [51]. The model employs the following assumptions for EDA generation process;
A1 Skin conductance responses are preceded by bursts from the sudomotor nerves controlling the
sweat glands.
These are temporally discrete episodes.
Skin conductance responses therefore
stems from a sparse neural signal which is non-negative due to the nature of a nerve activity.
A2 There is a linear relationship between the recruitment of sweat glands and the intensity of bursts.
Furthermore, the output response of the system solely depends on the application of a nerve input
at that particular instant, meaning that skin conductance responses are independent of previous
ones. Thus making it reasonable to characterise the system as a linear time-invariant system.
A3 The process of sweat diffusion has a participant-specific impulse response function, which is rela-
tively stable for all skin conductance responses of the same participant.
A4 The phasic component is superimposed to slowly varying tonic components with frequency spec-
trum below 0.05Hz, for representing the information content in samples at every 10 seconds.
We now model the physiological signal of skin conductance through the following observation model
y = r + h + ŽĄ
(5.1)
where y ŌłłRn is the physiological signal of skin conductance, r ŌłłRn is the phasic component, while
h ŌłłRn is the tonic component and ŽĄt
iid
Ōł╝N(0, Žā2) is the modelling and measurements errors.
We represent the tonic component as a sum of cubic B-splines with 10 seconds equidistant knots
(A4) alongside a bias (intercept) term and a linear term, as it has been done in the following
h = Al + Cd,
(5.2)
where A ŌłłRn├Św (where w here denotes the number of cubic basis-functions) is a matrix consisting of
cubic basis-functions, l ŌłłRw is a vector of spline coe’¼Ćicients, C ŌłłRn├Ś2 is a design-matrix for the bias
term and the linear term, while d ŌłłR2 is a vector of corresponding coe’¼Ćicients.

==PAGE:52==
5.2 Physiological Signals
43
5.2
Physiological Signals
The signals from the Empatica E4 require the need for pre-processing due to for instance noise such as
motion artifacts et cetera, in order to be suitable for the appropriate machine learning models.
We note from figure 4.2 that it ought to be sensible to apply a burn-in period for the signals, hence
using a 5 second burn-in period for all signals. We then employ a four-step pre-processing procedure;
Decomposition: The EDA signal has been decomposed into a phasic and a tonic component by using
the cvxEDA algorithm as proposed in [51] and implemented in [88] (see section 5.1 for details),
while simultaneously keeping the original EDA signal for additional analysis.
Filtering: The original EDA signal is filtered using a low-pass filter as proposed by [12, 108], such as for
instance the Butterworth filter. Here using a 4ŌĆ▓th order Butterworth filter with a cut-off frequency
of 1.5Hz in order to reduce the otherwise inherent motion artifacts [152].
Smoothing: A smoothing has been applied to the original EDA signal by an exponential smoothing to
reduce motion artifacts, as proposed by [12, 57]. This is done with a smoothing factor of ╬▒ = 0.4.
St = ╬▒yt + (1 ŌłÆ╬▒)StŌłÆ1 for t Ōłł{2, . . . , n}, S1 = y1.
(5.32)
Feature Extraction: A number of appropriate features are extracted for each signal. This is done
for each phase, in each round, for each participant. For a comprehensive overview of the features
under consideration please refer to table A.1.
Note that the signals of the acceleration have not been considered in this dissertation.
Proceeding the pre-processing procedure, we continue by creating two data-sets. The original, which
includes all features for each phase in each round (omitting the first round) for each participant, with
each of these constituting a single observation. The delta data-set, which is a baseline correction of the
original data-set, that is; the features from the first rest phase, id est phase 1, in round 2 constitute a
baseline for each participant. We thus correct the remaining observations by the subtraction of this.
Table 5.1: Overview of the two data-sets.
Data-set
n ├Ś p
Original
126 ├Ś 97
Delta
112 ├Ś 97
Note that the answers to the self-report questionnaires constitute the targets (excluding di’¼Ćiculty)
and that both data-sets have observations where the corresponding targets contain not-a-number (NaN)
entries, four for the original data-set and three for the delta data-set, hence excluding these observations.
==PAGE:53==
CHAPTER 6
Static Predictive Models
We will now give a thorough presentation of the static predictive models, which we intend to employ.
Both linear and non-linear models will be considered to assess different approaches.
We aspire to
perform emotion recognition by predicting the answers to the self-report questionnaires based upon the
physiological signals received during the experiment, hence this being a supervised regression problem.
The subsequent static predictive models are chosen due to being simple machine learning models,
while some include the property of feature importance [54]. Furthermore, we shall let the average of
the targets constitute a baseline (Dummy), for model comparison and selection purposes.
We will now start by revisiting the standard linear regression model with ordinary least squares (OLS).
6.1
Ordinary Least Squares
A standard linear regression model is a model on the form [86]
y = X╬▓ + ŽĄ,
(6.1)
where X ŌłłRn├Śp is an observation matrix with each row corresponding to an observation and each
