parameters, and ŽĄ is an n-dimensional, zero-mean stochastic error vector with a constant covariance
matrix [86]. Note that it is indeed possible to include a bias (intercept) by adding an auxiliary parameter.
This models leads itself to the following optimisation problem in the case of ordinary least squares;
this means using the quadratic loss-function when minimising the empirical risk, alongside the additional
assumption of the error terms being a white-noise process (confer section C.2).

6.2
Elastic-Net
Succeeding ordinary least squares, we will now consider the linear regression model; elastic-net (EN). This
is a linear regression model with regularisation, which generalises ridge regression and lasso regression

It is hereby seen that the regularisation imposed by elastic-net, constitutes a linear-combination of
the Ōäō1-norm and the Ōäō2-norm respectively. Thus inheriting both the property of feature importance
from lasso regression as well as the regularisation property from ridge regression [54, 131]. Elastic-net
also has the property of being able to work outside the constraint of p Ōēżn, and can here potentially
yield more than n non-zero parameters (unlike lasso regression) [54]. Furthermore, elastic-net provides
e’¼Ćicient regularisation of parameters associated with correlated features, as it encourages the averaging
of these parameters in order to group these together, while still maintaining feature importance [54].
Moreover, note that ╬▒ Ōłł[0, 1] controls the ratio of the the Ōäō1-norm and the Ōäō2-norm, while ╬╗ Ōēź0
controls the weight given to the penalty term. The optimisation problem posed in relation to elastic-
net can be resolved with the use of the coordinate descent algorithm. Note that elastic-net yields a
convex optimisation problem, hence having a global optimum [42]. The coordinate descent algorithm
successively optimises along coordinate directions to find the optimum. This can be done in a cyclic
manner or at random. The idea is now to optimise a multivariate optimisation problem by optimising
along one coordinate direction at a time, while keeping the others fixed, hence solving simpler univariate
optimisation problems until the convergence of the algorithm. The standard approach of coordinate
descent for elastic-net is as follows; assume that the observations are standardised, the update of the
algorithm is then given by the expression below, which is repeated until convergence [42, 54]

==PAGE:55==
6.3 Decision Trees
46
6.3
Decision Trees
Next, the commencement of non-parametric, non-linear approaches, which enable more complex models
and thus often superior performances. We will here start by considering decision trees (DT). This is a class
of simple non-parametric, non-linear supervised models, available for both regression and classification.
There exists various methodologies for the development of decision trees, although we will here examine
the popular method known as CART (classification and regression trees).
The underlying idea of a
decision tree is to infer simple decision-rules from the observations and use these to predict the targets.
We will here limit the discussion to the regression case. Consider the setting of supervised regression.
A decision tree recursively partitions the feature space in order to collect targets with similar values, it
then fits a constant in each partition thus yielding a piecewise constant approximation [54, 131].
Let Qm denote partition (or node) m with nm observations. For each potential split, consisting of
feature j and threshold tm, we divide the observations into the following two partitions [54, 131]
Ql = Qleft
m (j, tm) = {(xi, yi) | i Ōłł{1, . . . , nm} Ōł¦xij Ōēżtm},
(6.12)
Qr = Qright
m
(j, tm) = {(xi, yi) | i Ōłł{1, . . . , nm} Ōł¦xij > tm},
(6.13)
where Ōł¦denotes logical conjunction. We then assess the quality of the candidate split for partition m
by computing the empirical risk associated with some pre-specified loss-function (impurity measure).
The selection of the parameters is then specified in accordance with the following
inf
jŌłł{1,...,p},tmŌłłR
’Ż«
’Ż░nleft
m
nm
X
(xk,yl)ŌłłQl
L(f(xl), yl) + nright
m

X

L(f(xr), yr)
’Ż╣
’Ż╗,
(6.14)
where nleft
m and nright
m
are the cardinalities of the respective partitions. This is then repeated until some
convergence criteria is fulfilled, nm = 1 or nm < nmin (nmin being the minimum number of observations).
Regarding the loss-function; the most common criteria to determine a split is the mean-squared-error,
where f(x) = ym (that is the average of y in the partition m) [54, 131].
Having grown a complete tree, we can perform what is known as minimal cost-complexity pruning
in order to reduce (prune) the tree, as large trees are susceptible to overfitting [54, 131]. It now follows;
let T0 be the complete tree and define here a subtree T ŌŖéT0 as any pruning of the complete tree, id
est the collapsing of non-terminal nodes into terminal (leaf) nodes by removing the associated branch.
We now define a cost-complexity measure in the following [54, 131]
C╬▒(T) = C(T) + ╬▒| ╦£T| =
’Ż«
’Ż░
| ╦£T |
X

X
(xk,yk)ŌłłQv
L(f(xk), yk)
’Ż╣
’Ż╗+ ╬▒| ╦£T|,
(6.15)
where Qv is the set of examples in the terminal node v and | ╦£T| is the number of terminal nodes in the
subtree T. Further, note that the hyper-parameter ╬▒ ŌłłRŌēź0 governs the trade-off between the size of the
tree and the fit to the data [54]. The underlying idea is now to find the subtree T╬▒ ŌŖåT0 that minimises
the cost-complexity measure for each ╬▒. To find T╬▒ we utilise weakest link pruning; Successively prune
the non-terminal node that produces the smallest per-node increase in C(T) and continue till a single
node (root) constitutes the tree. The value of ╬▒ can be found by K-fold cross-validation [54].
A primary reason for considering decision trees is on account of the feature importance property.
The evaluation of the relative feature importance is done based upon the normalisation of the total
reduction in the empirical risk associated with the particular feature in question [54, 131].
An issue relating to decision trees is their high variance; small perturbations in the data, yields
significant different splits. This behaviour originates from the hierarchical nature of the process resulting
in the propagation of errors, leading to the instability of decision trees.
However, an approach for
diminishing this inherent instability through variance reduction will subsequently be considered [54].
==PAGE:56==
6.4 Random Forests
47
6.4
Random Forests
A random forest (RF) attempts to reduce the high variance and enhance the performance associated
with decision trees by utilising a modified version of bagging (also referred to as bootstrap aggregation).
A random forest is an ensemble method, which combines multiple decision trees to improve the results.
The technique of bagging (in the case of regression) constructs a number of data-sets with the same
size, using uniform sampling with replacement from the original data-set, and then fits the regression
models and averages the prediction [54]. For more information please consult section C.4.3. A random
forest does not use a standard bagging approach but rather a modification, as it makes a collection of
de-correlated decision trees and then averages the predictions. The algorithms follows subsequently.
Algorithm 3 Random Forests Algorithm.
1: Given Z ŌłłN estimators.
2: Given a minimum number of observations in a node, nmin.
3: Given a maximum number of features, pmax.
4: for z in 1 to Z do
5:
Sample data-set from original data-set using uniform sampling with replacement.
6:
repeat
Ō¢ĘFit a decision tree Tz to the data-set.
7:
Select pmax features at random from the original p features.
8:
Pick the best feature and split amongst the pmax features.
9:
Split the node.
10:
until nv < nmin.
11: end for
Consider the setting of Z decision trees acquired through bagging, hence these yield identically
distributed errors. Suppose that the errors for a specific example {ŽĄz}Z

It is hereby seen that as the number of decision trees increases, id est Z, the second term disappears.
However, the first term remains thus illustrating how the mutual correlation retains the benefits oth-
erwise achieved from bagging. The idea in a random forest is therefore to minimise the correlation in
order to reduce the variance and thus enhance the performance. This is done in the process of producing
the decision trees by selecting a random subset of the features prior to each split (see algorithm 3).
A recommendation is to use pmax = ŌīŖp/3Ōīŗfeatures (ŌīŖ┬ĘŌīŗrounds down to nearest integer) and a
minimum of 5 observation in a node, although in practice the specifics depend on the problem [54].
Random forests also inherit the property of feature importance from decision trees, although here
the improvement associated with each feature is accumulated across each tree [54].
Another important property of random forests are out-of-bag (OOB) errors, that is; for each example,
construct a random forest by only averaging decision trees in which the example does not appear [54].
This can then be utilised for continuous assessment, in order to for instance conclude upon the number
of decision trees [54]. We can likewise exploit this property for feature importance; for each example not
in the decision tree compute the empirical risk, then permute the respective feature across all relevant
observations and compute the empirical risk again. Average this reduction across all decision trees and
let it act as a measure of the feature importance for the respective feature [54].
==PAGE:57==
6.5 Gaussian Processes
48
Random forests are generally less likely to overfit compared to decision trees and generally yield
better performance, hence often being desirable albeit the computational complexity associated [54].
6.5
Gaussian Processes
An additional class of non-parametric, non-linear models are Gaussian processes (GP), which is a class
of supervised models applicable in both the setting of regression and classification. A Gaussian process
is a stochastic process (see section C.2), which can be seen as a distribution over functions [116]. Note
here that one can informally think of a function as an infinite long vector with each element specifying
a function evaluation at a given point. This makes Gaussian processes ideal for modelling functions.
Consider here the setting of regression. We now start by defining a Gaussian process [116].
Definition 6.5.1 (Gaussian Processes). A Gaussian process is a collection of stochastic variables for
which any finite number is a multivariate Gaussian distribution.
Let now the stochastic variables represent the value of the function f(x) at x. A Gaussian process
can be fully specified by its mean function m : Rp ŌåÆR and its covariance function k : Rp ├Ś Rp ŌåÆR.
For real-valued f(x), we define these as follows [116]
m(x) = E[f(x)].
(6.19)
k(x, xŌĆ▓) = E[(f(x) ŌłÆm(x))(f(xŌĆ▓) ŌłÆm(xŌĆ▓))].
(6.20)

6.5.1
Covariance Functions
The question now becomes, how do we construct the covariance matrix. The covariance function is
seen to be pivotal in Gaussian processes, as it incorporates the assumptions of the function which we
attempt to model. We will here therefore present some common covariance functions [116].
We start by presenting the requirement for a function to be a valid covariance function, which is
that it must be a positive definite kernel (PSD) [116]. The definition of which now follows [67].

6.6 k-Nearest Neighbours
Another non-parametric, non-linear model is k-nearest neighbours (KNN), which is available in the setting
of supervised models (both regression and classification) and unsupervised models. In the supervised
case the model exploits the underlying idea of targets being similar to those, of which the corresponding
observations are in close proximity. Hereby simply consists of finding the k ŌłłNŌēżn nearest neighbours of
each observation, according to some pre-specified norm, and then in the case of regression computing for
example the average or the median of the corresponding targets [115]. A common choice of norm is to
use either the Ōäō1-norm or the Ōäō2-norm. For details of k-nearest neighbours for regression see algorithm 4.
Algorithm 4 k-Nearest Neighbours for Regression.
1: Pick a value k ŌłłNŌēżn.
2: for i in 1 to n do
3:

5:

6:
Sort the distances in descending order.
7:
Save the indices of the observations from the first k distances in Ii.
8:
Take an average of the k targets; ╦åyi = 1
k
P
lŌłłIi yl.
9: end for
Note that a satisfactory value of k can be found by the use of K-fold cross-validation [56], there
exists multiple algorithms for finding the k-nearest neighbours, although the most basic approach is
simply to use brute-force. However, more sophisticated algorithms are available and for these please
refer to [131]. A modification of the standard k-nearest neighbour algorithm can be done by weighing
the targets according to the distance of the corresponding observations with closer observations having a
larger weight and vice versa. A popular weighting scheme is (assuming a positive distance) to weight by
the inverse distance or the squared inverse distance [115]. Additional details can be found in algorithm 5.

==PAGE:61==
CHAPTER 7
Results
This chapter will present the results attained in relation to research question; R1, R2 and R3. This will
be done by first providing an analysis of BVP with reference to research question R3, and then afterwards
presenting the results we have acquired on account of research question R2 and R3 respectively.
7.1
Analysis of BVP
The physiological signal of BVP is to a far greater extent prone to noise compared to other signals. The
reasons for this include, but are not limited to; motion, lack of tightness of measurement device causing
inconsistent illumination, interference of external light sources, participant dependent characteristics
such as varying skin colour, skin structure, et cetera [12, 144]. There is evidently a lot of uncertainty
and shortcomings associated with the measurement of BVP yielding di’¼Ćiculties in utilising the signal.
This is a well-known issue, which has been widely discussed within the field of research [12, 130],
hence why this has been of particular interest and the subject of great scrutiny. A primary focus of
this dissertation has therefore been to assess the usability of the BVP signal with reference to emotion
recognition in regards to the set of pre-specified features. The pre-processing of BVP has therefore been a
topic of significant interest, albeit the results of this being of little to no avail, hence instead considering
the circumstances and criteria for which BVP yields itself to the features in question being applicable.
This has been done through the following experiment. We start by simulating a BVP signal with a
heart-rate of 70 beats-per-minute, a sample-rate of 64Hz and a duration of 300 seconds, similar to that
of the pilot-study experiment, using the implementation made available in [88] and extract the features.
A simulation of a BVP signal can be seen in figure A.1. We then add various sources of noise to the
signal in an incremental fashion, that is; we achieve three different versions of the same signal identified
by the addition of different noise sources. The noise altering procedure can be found in the following;
1. Add white-noise in 0.5 second increments from the start of the signal with 550 increments in total.
2. Add segments of high-frequency noise (the cut-off is 20Hz) to random positions of version 1.
3. Add high-intensity impulses (with probability p = 0.1) to random positions of version 2.
The selection of the above noise sources have been done in accordance with the noise present in the BVP
signal acquired during the pilot-study experiment in order to achieve a thorough representation.
An example of a BVP simulation with different noise sources can be found in figure A.2.
We finally visualise this in figure 7.1 by plotting the absolute difference between the original features
to the noise altered features as a function of the signal-to-noise-ratio id est SNR = ┬Ą/Žā [150].
Note that the slope features here use a window-size of 50 seconds and a window-shift of 25 seconds.
==PAGE:62==

A positive value indicates that the method on average is closer to the feature from the original simu-
lation and vice versa. It is hereby seen that at specific signal-to-noise-ratios we obtain no improvement
for some of the features, albeit doing so for others which is in accordance with figure 7.2. Furthermore,
in order to assess if the segmentation method attains better results on average than the standard signal-
processing approach, we perform dependent two-sample t-tests (confer section 3.2.4). The results of
which can be found in table 7.2. Performing 88 hypothesis tests yield for the pre-specified significance
level of ╬▒ = 0.05, a family-wise error rate of approximately 0.989. Therefore, to prevent the problem of
multiple hypothesis testing we adopt the Bonferroni correction (please refer to section 3.1.1 for details).
This yields a corrected significance level of approximately 0.0006.

At last, we also established an experiment to assess whether or not it would indeed be possible to
conclude upon a satisfactory segment-size according to the associated differences. This was done by
varying the segment-size and inspecting the corresponding differences, yet to no success. The reason for
this experiment being that a larger segment-size could conceivably enable the acquisition of more infor-
mation at lower frequencies, whereas a smaller segment-size would yield an adequate representation at
larger frequencies. However, the results (see figure A.5) did not provide grounds for a general conclusion.
LetŌĆÖs proceed by presenting the results of the emotion recognition for research question R1 and R2.
==PAGE:66==
7.2 Emotion Recognition
57
7.2
Emotion Recognition
First, to address the problem of multiple hypothesis testing arising from the multi-dimensional nature of
the targets, we perform a principal component analysis to examine the prospect of a lower-dimensional
representations of the targets, such to avoid this otherwise inherent problem. Moreover, by compressing
the targets we may also alleviate any potential challenges resulting from having few participants as
well as low consistency within the targets, due to the subjective experience of each participant. In
addition to this, it is seen from figure 7.3 that some of the targets are indeed correlated [132] and
thus represent redundant information. This ought to be expected as the relevant emotional states are
comparable, hence yielding similar results in the self-report questionnaires, especially for participants
who for instance have di’¼Ćiculty distinguishing their emotions from one another.
Figure 7.3: Graphical heat-map representation of the correlation matrix of the targets. The values
represent the p-values for testing the null-hypothesis H0 : Žüij = 0 against the alternative HA : Žüij ╠Ė= 0.
We perform a principal component analysis for which a graphical analysis is available in figure 7.4.
==PAGE:67==
7.2 Emotion Recognition
58
Figure 7.4: Principal component analysis of the targets (the ellipsoid uses 3 standard deviations).
Figure 7.4 shows that we ought to retain the first two principal components. Moreover, we also
observe that the first principal component primarily explains targets of emotions associated with
neutral valence and high arousal, while the second principal component to some degree corresponds
to targets of emotions relating to slightly more negative valence and of more neutral arousal [122].
Figure 7.5: RusselŌĆÖs circumplex model.
To state the aforesaid in simpler terms; the first principal
component explains the axis of arousal (the 2ŌĆÖnd axis) in
RusselŌĆÖs circumplex model (see figure 7.5 for an illustration)
and the second principal component approximately corre-
sponds to the axis of valence (the 1ŌĆÖst axis) [122].
The resulting lower-dimensional representation of the
targets using the two first principal components can now
be found in figure 7.6, where it pre-eminently can be seen
how participants differ within themselves as well as between
each other in the lower-dimensional representation.
==PAGE:68==
7.2 Emotion Recognition
59
Figure 7.6: Lower-dimensional representation of the targets.
A number of different static predictive models (confer section 6) have hereafter been applied in order
to assess the feasibility of emotion recognition by predicting the lower-dimensional representation of the
targets using the features derived from the physiological signals. Likewise, enabling the evaluation of
feature importances. We here employ a leave-one-participant-out cross-validation scheme due to the
limitation on available data, omitting participant 14 for testing. Note that we in the following also
apply a simple feed-forward neural-network (FFNN) (please refer to section C.4 for more information).
The features are subsequently standardised by reasons of applying elastic-net, thus it being imperative
in order to penalise each parameter alike. Similar for k-nearest neighbours, as this is a distance-based
approach such that no single feature dominates the distance measure, and instead having each feature
contributing equally. Likewise, for Gaussian processes it may also be good practice as the covariance
functions are often distance-based. In regards to the feed-forward neural-network please see section C.4.
The features are standardised within each cross-validation fold to ensure no carry over of information.
A presentation of the respective hyper-parameters of the models is available in table 7.3, here using the
models made accessible in scikit-learn (the reader is referred to [131] for a comprehensive overview).
==PAGE:69==

We observe from figure 7.7a that the models are not able to learn from the original data-set, as seen
from the training-errors being larger than the validation-errors, hence indicating that the data resembles
white-noise or that the models are unable to capture the underlying structural variation within the data.
Note that the limitation on available data may contribute to/be part of the reason for these shortcomings.
We therefore discard the original data-set for the remaining of this section. It is likewise seen from figure
7.7b that some of the models are not able to generalise well to unseen participants due to the relatively
large variation within the validation-errors indicating high inter-participant variability.

7.3 Personalised Models

7.3
Personalised Models
It is seen from figure 7.7b that the high inter-participant variability is perhaps one of the reasons
that the (participant independent) models are not able to achieve adequate generalisable properties,
which prevents the models from obtaining satisfactory performance. We therefore propose and present
personalised models to assess the performance of participant dependent models. We shall here restrict
the consideration to participant 1. This is done using a leave-one-out cross-validation scheme, omitting
the last observation for testing. The hyper-parameters of the models are available in table 7.10.

7.4
Incorporation of BVP
We have in section 7.1 as well as section 8.1 established several good practices for working with the
physiological signal of BVP in the presence of noise, when in the context of feature extraction for machine
learning models. It thus makes sense to utilise these observations to incorporate BVP as the average
signal-to-noise-ratio of the acquired BVP signals (after applying the burn-in period) is: 0.00037 Ōēł0.
Therefore including the features; the time average, the time median, the integral, the minimum frequency
and the maximum frequency, for the physiological signal of BVP. We present the results in section A.
==PAGE:79==
CHAPTER 8
Discussion
We will here provide a thorough discussion of the results from section 7 with reference to the research
questions, which we originally proposed in the introduction.
We shall start by addressing research
question R3, proceeding by R1 and R2.
8.1
Prospects of BVP
We have for the physiological signal of BVP carried out an empirical analysis with the objective of
quantifying the influence of noise on different features of BVP, while additionally proposing a novel
method for circumventing such influences, diverging from standard signal-processing approaches which
are otherwise predominant within the respective field of research (see for instance [12, 81, 124, 152])
To assess the prospects of BVP we start by providing an initial assessment of the influence of noise
for a number of different features. By consulting figure 7.1 and figure A.3 it is seen that;
ŌĆó The signal-to-noise-ratio appears to have immense influence on the quality of the features.
ŌĆó For well-behaved signals, id est SNR > 1, all features except the sum of frequencies, the average
frequency, the real part of the median frequency, the time minimum, the time maximum and the
average gradient are suitable for emotion recognition. While, the maximum slope and the slope
ought to be the subject of great scrutiny as the established errors associated vary in magnitude.
ŌĆó For acceptable signals, id est SNR Ōłł[0.5, 1], a subset of features are still sensible for emotion
recognition, albeit the need for reluctance in regards to the features of the minimum slope, the
average slope as well as the interquantile range of the frequencies.
ŌĆó For substandard signals, id est SNR < 0.5, most features are not reasonable to exploit and one
should thus consider discarding the signal. However, if necessary one may (with caution) consider
using the features; the time average, the time median, the integral, the minimum frequency, the
maximum frequency as well as the average negative gradient.
These observations constitute the circumstances and criteria for which the different features of BVP
are appropriate for emotion recognition. Note that one should be reluctant to conclude upon these
circumstances and criteria from version 1 of the noise altering procedure as this version may not depict
real-life noise sources adequately, hence why a more hesitant ŌĆØworst-caseŌĆØ approach is deemed necessary
and the reason for considering version 3. Furthermore, be aware that the most important characteristics
of the BVP signal is the frequency and the morphology, and not the intensity [144]. Note on account of
the aforementioned observations, that the time features may perhaps appear to have a predisposition
to robustness of noise altercations in contrast to the frequency counterparts.
We likewise identify from figure 7.2 in addition with figure A.4 that;
ŌĆó In general, the segmentation method appears to produce an improvement in the results, except
for the features; the integral, the slope, the minimum frequency and the maximum frequency.
ŌĆó The segmentation method yields similar performance to the standard signal-processing approach.
==PAGE:80==
8.2 Feasibility of Emotion Recognition
71
The reason for the poor performance of the features in question ought to be on account of the signal being
discontinuous. Thus making the features comprise a linear combination of features originating from each
individual segment, under which condition it is not necessarily sensible to evaluate the aforementioned
features.
Hereby also changing the circumstances and basis for which we compare the features, as
these features now arise differently from the original features which they are indeed compared to, thus
yielding a discrepancy. Ergo asserting that the method is not applicable for these specific features, to
prevent the attainment of unreasonable results which may otherwise be the case.
Furthermore, it is from table 7.2 evident that the segmentation method on average performs signif-
icantly better than the standard signal-processing approach for a subset of the features at the specific
values of the signal-to-noise-ratio. Overall, it is seen that these features consist of all the time features
except the slope as well as a subset of the frequency features including; the real part of the sum of
frequencies (except for SNR = 0.10), the imaginary part of the sum of frequencies, the interquantile
range of the frequencies as well as the imaginary part of the average of the frequencies.
The segmentation method is hereby seen to provide promising results with the method in some cases
outperforming the standard signal-processing approach on average, as seen from table 7.2. However,
the segmentation method is based upon the underlying assumption of explicitly knowing the positions
of the noise, which may not be reasonable nor attainable in a real-life setting, hence being a significant
limitation. We shall for this reason propose extensions to the segmentation method for the advancement
of practicality, which we will do in section 9.1. The observations from this analysis thus facilitate good
practices for the physiological signal of BVP in the setting of machine learning models where feature
extraction constitutes a necessity.
8.2
Feasibility of Emotion Recognition
We have for both the original data-set and the delta data-set made a complete analysis of predictive
emotion recognition by employing various machine learning models.
Here starting by training the
models across different participants. The results are available in figure 7.7, from which it is seen that;
ŌĆó The models are not able to learn when using the original data-set.
ŌĆó The delta data-set allows the models to learn, albeit some yielding relatively high variation within
the validation-errors implying high inter-participant variability.
These two observations constitute the grounds for emotion recognition in this current state not being
ideal with respect to achieving satisfactory performance as well as producing robust models capable
of generalising to unseen participants.
This is despite the fact that a major issue throughout the
dissertation has been to decrease the variance at the cost of increasing the bias to avoid overfitting,
such to gain better generalisable properties. Here the lack of available data has been the subject of
great concern, as it enforces the construction of particularly simple models as well as limiting both the
statistical power and the generalisability. Furthermore, it is seen from table 7.5 that we are not able to
conclude upon the best model based upon statistical arguments as the models do not yield significantly
different performances, albeit elastic-net producing the smallest value of the empirical risk.
In addition to this, we also provide an analysis of the feature importances as well as their robustness.
By first addressing figure 7.9, it is seen that only a single feature of the physiological signal of TEMP is of
importance, more specifically the integral, while no features are of importance for EDA. It is here also seen
that the physiological signal of HR as well as the phasic component are both of particular importance
with the features of these dominating the relative importances of larger magnitudes. This compares well
with what we might expect as both HR and the phasic component resemble fast physiological changes
originating from short-time responses to stimuli, which ought to provide the predominant physiological
changes due to the experimental setup of the pilot-study. Further, note the remarkable importance of the
integral features as evident in figure 7.9. This matches the findings of [139] (analysing EDA). The integral
here acts as a type of offset representing the size, exposure and measurement time of the physiological
signals [103]. The time average of HR is also found to be of great importance as it is also the case in [129],
==PAGE:81==
8.2 Feasibility of Emotion Recognition
72
hereby making it reasonable that the time median is also of importance as these two features are both
measures of central tendency. However, several discrepancies also materialise as some of the features
here differ from those found in the literature (such as for instance [129, 139]). This is for example
the number of peaks of the phasic component [139] and the time minimum/maximum of TEMP [129],
hence indicating that the features of importance are perhaps domain/case specific or inconsistent. We
therefore provide a supplementary assessment of the robustness of the different features of importance
due to the aforesaid. It is seen in figure 7.9 and figure 7.10 that the features of importance during
testing coincide with the features of importance which are also consistent throughout training, with the
features of considerable importance here having a count larger than 10. The feature importances and
corresponding consistencies ought to be taking into account in the development of future models.
In order to assess the possibility of more robust models enabling the acquisition of better generalis-
able properties we consider personalised models. This is done in an effort to circumvent the intrinsic
problem of relatively high inter-participant variability, which perhaps is obstructing the performance of
the participant independent models by yielding uncertainty in the predictive properties. However, this
approach was deemed infeasible due to the limitation on available data. Another approach is therefore
to instead combine the participant independent models with the participant dependent models in order
to (hopefully) obtain superior performance to those of the distinct models. Here most of the models are
able to learn in both the setting of the original data-set and the delta data-set, which has not previously
been the case, thus providing promising results. Furthermore, it is seen that the random forest produces
the best results for both data-sets, while it performs significantly better than the baseline for the delta
data-set. Despite this, it is evident from figure 7.12 that we are unable to attain notable improvements
for the variation within the validation-errors.
In regards to the feature importances it is seen that the features from the physiological signals of
HR and the phasic component are dominating the feature importances for both data-sets, while the
physiological signal of EDA and the tonic component are also of importance for the original data-set.
Hereby indicating that some features are better within the original domain rather than the baseline
corrected domain and vice versa. A more complex model may therefore perhaps utilise a combination
of these two domains. Note that there here is little to no correspondence to the features of importance
found in [129, 139], further indicating the possibility of the important features being domain/case
specific. Discrepancies between the feature importances and their robustness also transpire, with for
instance the time median of the phasic component being important for the original data-set, albeit not
being consistent. Likewise, for the delta data-set with exempli gratia the time standard deviation of
HR. However, the important features are predominantly consistent within the data-sets. Note also that
for instance the integral of HR is consistent within the delta data-set despite not being an important
feature. We also observe that some features such as the time average of HR is not important here, while
being so for the independent models, thus highlighting the fact that feature importances differ between
the two approaches.
We have here also made an attempt at implementing a K-fold cross-validation scheme, employing
a coarse grid-search within each fold for hyper-parameter optimisation and then an exhaustive grid-
search for the final model. However, this did not yield any results of superior quality, hence excluding
this proposition.
Note that other hyper-parameter optimisation approaches such as for example a
randomised search (confer [48, 131]) would indeed also be viable option.
In regards to general observations for the different settings of the analysis; it is seen that a Gaussian
process is generally not a sensible model choice, as it appears to not be able learn from the data.
This is perhaps due to a constant covariance function being too much of simplification thus yielding
unreasonable assumptions, although other more complex covariance functions were tried these lead to
overfitting.
It is also seen that the models are generally not able to predict the second dimension
well. This demonstrates a lack of relationship between the features and this dimension. It could also
be an inadequacy in the models, although as this behaviour was present for both elastic-net and the
random forest this ought to be improbable. However, note from figure A.9a and figure A.10a that the
behaviour is more well-behaved for the training and the validation. This may partly stem from the
correspondence between the targets and the second principal component being less prevalent than that
of the first principal component as seen in figure 7.4.
==PAGE:82==
8.2 Feasibility of Emotion Recognition
73
We have also incorporated a subset of BVP features by consulting the observations from section 8.1.
However, due to the poor quality of the signals this only leads to the incorporation of five additional
features, which did not provide a notable difference in the performance. Hereby indicating the need for
further work in order to assess the potential as well as the importance of incorporating BVP, as this may
be vital to the development of a successful emotion recognition system. Furthermore, of the included
BVP features only the time median is both important as well as consistent, for the original data-set.
This is in spite of additional BVP features being consistent during training.
It is important to note for the previous considerations that the data originates from two distinct
trials with different conditions. An example of this is for instance differences in temperature, which
are readily seen from the skin temperature of the participants. This ought to yield discrepancies in the
data, which may add to the already existing problem of not being able to achieve adequate generalisable
properties. This is perhaps also one of the reasons that the delta data-set yields better performance,
as a baseline correction may reduce the influence of such discrepancies. However, one may argue that
in order for emotion recognition to be feasible in real-life that the models must be able to take such
discrepancies into account. Yet, this may be possible in future settings with more data being available,
allowing the development of more sophisticated models. Furthermore, note that the above discussion
concerns the respective data-sets, hence being restricted by the amount of available data constituting
a well-established limitation.
==PAGE:83==
CHAPTER 9
Conclusion
The goal of this dissertation has been to develop and apply state-of-the-art methods for acquiring
insights into the field of emotion recognition using physiological signals from wearable devices.
To provide a foundation for this work we therefore started by studying the relevant literature to
obtain a comprehensive overview of the current field of research. We proceeded by establishing a sound
theoretical background within the mathematical topics of relevance as well as assessing domain specific
elements of emotion recognition.
By analysing the behaviour of the physiological signal of BVP in the presence of noise, we were able
to develop a framework consisting of simple criteria, for which it is feasible to exploit the different
features under consideration. We were additionally able to devise a novel feature extraction method
in the setting of noise, diverging from standard signal-processing approaches. The method provides
promising results as it on average outperforms a standard signal-processing approach for a subset of
time and frequency features.
Upon the completion of the pre-processing procedure and having thoroughly described the models
of interest, we proceeded by performing emotion recognition using the data of the pilot-study. We here
undertook an assortment of approaches. First, a participant independent approach, which lead itself
to elastic-net producing the smallest value of the empirical risk. Yet, high inter-participant variability
inhibited the generalisable properties of some of the models by causing undesirable variation within the
validation-errors. Second, inspired by for instance [129], we explored a participant dependent approach
in the form of personalised models. However, this was deemed infeasible due to the lack of data. As an
alternative we therefore proposed the use of a leave-one-out cross-validation scheme with all available
participants to ensure an adequate compromise between the two settings. This materialised itself in
most of the models being able to learn from both the original data-set as well as the delta data-set.
Moreover, the random forest proved to yield the best performance in both cases, while being significantly
better than the baseline for the delta data-set. Despite not being significantly better than elastic-net.
The sensitivity of the physiological signals caused high inter-participants variability for some of
the models, which was a recurring issue leading to di’¼Ćiculties in attaining satisfactory generalisable
properties. It was also found that hyper-parameter optimisation is not advisable due to the aforesaid
reasons as well as the limitation on available data. Overall, the limitation on available data has been
a consistent limiting factor throughout the dissertation. The incorporation of BVP features using the
designed framework did also not allow for noteworthy differences due to the poor quality of the signals.
By analysing different features on the basis of providing insights into feature importances as well
as their robustness for future model development, we found that feature importances differ across
approaches and domains. That the physiological signal of HR as well as the phasic component dominates
the feature importances, with the physiological signal of EDA as well as the tonic component also
being important for the original data-set. That a recurring feature which is both important as well as
consistent across domains and approaches being the number of peaks for the phasic component, hence
demonstrating global characteristics.
To summarise our findings;
ŌĆó We have established a random forest for performing emotion recognition based upon physiological
signals from the Empatica E4, which succeeds in achieving better performance than the baseline.
ŌĆó The extent to which we have been able to develop a machine learning model for emotion recognition
based on physiological signals from the Empatica E4 has been limited with the achieved results
==PAGE:84==
9.1 Future Work
75
certainly having both the potential as well as the need for improvements.
ŌĆó Emotion recognition using physiological signals is characterised by the sensitivity of physiological
signals causing high inter-participant variability leading to di’¼Ćiculties in obtaining robust models.
ŌĆó The features of the physiological signal of HR and the phasic component are the most prominent.
ŌĆó We have found that feature importances differ across domains and approaches.
ŌĆó We have developed a framework of simple criteria for using the physiological signal of BVP based
on the value of the signal-to-noise-ratio as well as devised a novel feature extraction method.
ŌĆó We have not been able to incorporate BVP effectively into the machine learning models for emotion
recognition due to the poor quality of the signals.
9.1
Future Work
We will now present a number of potential future directions to explore as well as extension to the work
done throughout this dissertation. The following consists of ideas and approaches for future work;
Features
A natural extension to the work done in the dissertation would be to make a more thorough inspection
of features. We have in this dissertation restricted the analysis to consist of a pre-specified subset of
features, albeit the extraction of additional features being feasible. This also extends to the analysis of
BVP, where it indeed would be possible to include more features of interest. Moreover, we discarded the
signal of acceleration and including it could perhaps provide vital information in regards to times of
motion where other physiological signals may be corrupted by noise, hence providing additional infor-
mation which can be incorporated in the filtering of for instance BVP, as it has been proposed by [123].
It would perhaps also be sensible to fuse the real part and imaginary part of the frequency features by
computing the absolute value (modulus) of the respective complex numbers.
Clinical Data
The data considered in this dissertation originates from a pilot-study, hence restricting the results
to only being applicable within the confinement of the specific ambulatory settings for which the ac-
quisition of data has taken place. Otherwise data drifting may occur yielding unreliable results. This
constitutes an undesirable restriction as the objective is to help children and adolescent with obsessive-
compulsive-disorder outside of therapy. Thus emphasising the need for a comprehensive analysis of
clinical data from an appropriate research design in order to accomplish the goal of the research. Please
refer to section B for an overview of the experimental setup of the clinical trial.
Deep Learning
We have in this dissertation primarily considered simple machine learning models. However, we may
also consider the approach of more sophisticated deep learning models. This can for example be done by
using the original physiological signals and then exploiting the properties of representational learning,
hence avoiding the need for explicit feature extraction altogether. One approach could be to use a
convolutional neural-network, as for instance proposed by [128]. This also allows for the combination
with other neural-networks such as a long short-term memory neural-network [128].
Transfer Learning
Another venture could be to investigate the potential of incorporating transfer learning [154]. This
==PAGE:85==
9.2 Learning Outcomes
76
could be done to transfer information across cohorts, between similar participants in the setting of
personalised models as well as for the future prospect of transferring between ambulatory settings and
real-life [37]. However, the limitation on available data and the high inter-participant variability ought
to introduce complications, hence producing additional obstacles to solve [37].
The Segmentation Method
Another important consideration is to present potential solutions to the intrinsic problem affecting
the segmentation method, specifically the requirement for the exact positions of the noise. One ap-
proach for expanding the method in order to circumvent this problem as well as conceivably improving
the performance, would be to develop a more complex algorithm. A suggestion of this is the following;
1. Use a sliding window with suitable specifications of the window-size and window-shift in order to
extract segments from the signal.
2. Assess which segments are appropriate according to a measure of fit. An example of this is found
in the study of [36], which put forward the skewness as a valid index of quality for discriminating
between different standards of BVP. Keep relevant segments and exclude the rest.
3. Compute the features of each segment and represent these by an average.
Notice that it is possible to combine this algorithm with a standard signal-processing approach by fil-
tering the physiological signal after determining the segments to retain.
The above proposals constitute a number of potential approaches for future work, although they do not
provide an exhaustive list and future work of this dissertation is therefore not limited to the above.
9.2
Learning Outcomes
We here provide a brief evaluation of the learning outcomes for the dissertation to provide perspective.
ŌĆó Is able to identify and reflect on technical scientific issues as well as understand the
interaction between various components which make up an issue.
We develop machine learning models for emotion recognition and analyse different modelling
approaches, while considering these in an interdisciplinary perspective. We investigate the noise
problem of the physiological signal of BVP and propose various solution approaches, while also
inspecting how one of these solution approaches influences emotion recognition.
ŌĆó Is able to apply elements of current research at international level to develop ideas
and solve problems.
We study and apply state-of-the-art methods for emotion recognition using physiological signals
from wearable devices as well as signal-processing methods for physiological signal.
ŌĆó Masters technical scientific methodologies, theories and tools as well as being able to
delimit a complex, open issue, see it in a broader academic and societal perspective,
and on this basis, propose a variety of actions.
We delimit the interdisciplinary problem of real-time classification of OCD-related anxiety to a
number of preliminary studies and view this in the relevant context for well-informed decision
making, both in regards to the BVP noise problem as well as the emotion recognition. This is done
while showing a comprehensive understanding of the theoretical and practical foundation.
ŌĆó Is able to develop relevant models for solving technical problems.
We consider appropriate machine learning models in order to perform emotion recognition and
devise the so-called segmentation method for feature extraction of BVP in the setting of noise.
==PAGE:86==
9.2 Learning Outcomes
77
ŌĆó Is able to seek out leading international research within the area of specialisation.
International research and state-of-the-art methods constitute intrinsic parts of the dissertation.

==PAGE:102==
APPENDIX B
Clinical Trial
We will here present supplementary details for the clinical trial; Wrist Angel: A Wearable AI Feedback
Tool for OCD Treatment and Research, by the mental health services in the Capital Region, Denmark.
This is an observational case control study, which aims to improve the assessment and psychotherapy
for children and adolescents with obsessive-compulsive-disorder through the development of an artifi-
cial intelligence tool for supporting patients, parents and therapists in cognitive behavioral therapy [82].
Additionally giving rise to innovation in the public health sector through the integration of wearable
devices and machine learning. The background for the trial is the stagnation of psychiatric research
due to time limitations and inherent human bias. Thus the need for long-term collaboration between
researchers within psychiatry and researcher/developers within technology, for the advancement of psy-
chiatry [82]. The results of the trial ought to be the development and implementation of an artificial
intelligence tool for the enhancement of therapy through the use of technology in the public health
sector. The trial will additionally advance research within computational science and psychiatry for
testing biomarkers of clinical relevance [82].
We will here start by providing an introduction to the experimental setup of the clinical trial [82].
B.1
Experimental Setup
An experiment is done to evaluate the feasibility and acceptability (for patients, parents and therapists)
of the implementation of digital tools in a communal mental health facility for children and adolescents.
This is done through the passive collection (using the Empatica E4) of physiological data indicating
stress, for the use of privacy preserving signal-processing and machine learning to predict episodes
relating to obsessive-compulsive-disorder in the patient and to assist the behaviour of parents as well as
to extract physiological features of importance for therapeutic response [82]. Furthermore, the active
collection of clinical and biochemical data such as oxytocin from both patient and parent has been done,
as it constitutes a biomarker for family accommodation [82].
The study population of the experiment comprise of 10 patients of all sexes, ranging from 8 to 17
years of age, receiving treatment at a communal mental health facility for children and adolescents,
alongside one parent. A control group consisting of 10 typically developing (response) children and
adolescents of corresponding sex and age (┬▒3 months) alongside a (response) parent, from the same
geographical area, has also been established to assess and examine the distinguishable properties [82].
The respective inclusion and exclusion criteria for the experiment are as follows
Inclusion:
ŌĆó Patient has obsessive-compulsive-disorder as primary or secondary diagnosis. Verification by semi-
structured psychopathological interview using the Kiddie Schedule for affective disorders and
schizophrenia for school-age children - present and lifetime versions (K-SADS-PL).
ŌĆó Patient has a rating of more than 7 points on the childrenŌĆÖs Yale-Brown obsessive-compulsive scale
(CY-BOC). The severities are; mild (8-15), moderate (16-23), severe (24-31) and extreme (32-40).
ŌĆó Patient is eligible for care in psychiatry for primary diagnosis, by determination of a psychiatrist.
==PAGE:103==
B.1 Experimental Setup
94
ŌĆó Patient is age 8 through 17 years.
ŌĆó Patient has signed the informed consent.
Exclusion:
ŌĆó Patient is participating in other trials relating to obsessive-compulsive-disorder.
ŌĆó Patient has comorbid illness, which contraindicates trial participation; pervasive development
disorder not including AspergerŌĆÖs syndrome, schizophrenia/paranoid psychosis, mania or bipolar
disorder, depressive psychotic disorder or substance dependence syndrome
ŌĆó Patient has an intelligence quotient (IQ) less than 70 points.
ŌĆó Patient or parent are unable to wear the Empatica E4 due to health conditions.
We now provide an overview in table B.1 of the interventions for each group in the experiment [82].
==PAGE:104==
B.1 Experimental Setup
95
Table B.1: Experiment intervention overview.
Group
Intervention
Patient
Will wear the Empatica E4 and press the event tag button when feeling
anxious or distressed.
Will use the application CARP for ecological momentary assessment (EMA)
to evaluate the emotional state, at random times throughout the day. This
will be done for data labelling purposes.
It also contains forms for weekly
documentation of obsessive-compulsive-disorder relating symptoms as well as
a guided exposure and response prevention form.
Will receive treatment-as-usual (TAU) at the mental health facility.
Will be offered an exposure and prevention session in week 0 and 8.
Control
Will wear the Empatica E4 and press the event tag button when feeling
anxious or distressed.
Will use the application CARP for ecological momentary assessment (EMA)
to evaluate the emotional state, at random times throughout the day. This
will be done for data labelling purposes.
It also contains forms for weekly
documentation of obsessive-compulsive-disorder relating symptoms as well as
a guided exposure and response prevention form.
Parent of patient
Will wear the Empatica E4 and press the event tag button when noticing their
child are feeling anxious or distressed.
Will use the application CARP for ecological momentary assessment (EMA)
to evaluate the emotional state, at random times throughout the day. This
will be done for data labelling purposes.
It also contains forms for weekly
documentation of obsessive-compulsive-disorder relating symptoms as well as
a guided exposure and response prevention form.
Parent of control
Will wear the Empatica E4 and press the event tag button when noticing their
child are feeling anxious or distressed.
Will use the application CARP for ecological momentary assessment (EMA)
to evaluate the emotional state, at random times throughout the day. This
will be done for data labelling purposes.
It also contains forms for weekly
documentation of obsessive-compulsive-disorder relating symptoms as well as
a guided exposure and response prevention form.
The experiment uses numerous outcome measures for evaluation. A list follows (for details see [82]).
Primary: The changes in the childrenŌĆÖs Yale-Brown obsessive-compulsive scale, from week ŌłÆ1 to 8.
==PAGE:105==
B.1 Experimental Setup
96
Secondary: The changes in the KIDSCREEN-52, from week 0 to 8.
Others:
ŌĆó The clinical global impression severity (CGI-S) and the clinical global impression improvement
(CGI-I), for week 0 through 8.

APPENDIX C
Additional Theory
We will here provide additional insight into the theory and concepts within the mathematical subjects,
relating to the statistics and machine learning utilised throughout this dissertation.
Bayesian Statistics Proceeding is an introduction to different inference and estimation methods,
which includes a paraphrasing of the Bayesian paradigm in the field of statistics and how it
differs from the classical counterpart. The main references will here be the classical work [10, 86].
Stochastic Processes A time series is a realisation of a stochastic process, hence time series analysis
is to an extent concerns the study as well as modelling of stochastic processes, as these contribute
the underlying assumptions and theory for which we are trying to establish a model. The study
of stochastic processes thus constitute an indispensable aspect of time series analysis, which we
ought to investigate thoroughly in order to achieve a comprehensive understanding of the subject;
time series analysis. The primary source for this section is the classical textbook [86].
Linear Models In advance of more complex models, we shall start by providing a short recap of the
most elementary, although despite this useful, models used in time series analysis namely the
linear models. These are simple and enable for an initial analysis of the system dynamics, thus
providing convenient insight into other model approaches or perhaps extensions of current models.
The main reference for this section will be [86].
Neural-Networks Lastly we consider neural-networks which constitute a class of non-linear black box
models within machine learning. Considered to be unprecedented in terms of flexibility due to the
non-linear nature, hence the reason for multiple contributions towards new results within artificial
intelligence. The references are mainly the textbooks [10, 48] backed by the articles [62, 141].
Note, like the preliminaries, that the contents of this chapter will mostly consist of univariate theory,
although most of the elements considered can be generalised to the corresponding multivariate case.
LetŌĆÖs now present Bayesian statistics and the problem of Bayesian inference, both of which differ from
the classical approach. We will therefore throughout this section establish and compare the Bayesian
approach to the classical approach in order to highlight their differences as well as similarities.
==PAGE:107==
C.1 Bayesian Statistics
98
C.1
Bayesian Statistics
In the field of statistics there is often considered to be two different paradigms, namely the classical
(or frequentist) approach or interpretation of probability, where the probability of an event is defined
as the asymptotic frequency when performing trials of said event. Consider here the classical example
of a fair regular six-sided die. This has the probability of 1/6 of landing on each of its side, because if
we keep rolling the die then on average we will get each number 1/6 of the times, and the probability
hereby follows from the law of large numbers [69]. This is the classical interpretation of the probability
of an event. However, what is the probability of an event which does not have multiple occurrences and
thus perhaps fall outside the scope of the classical interpretation, as an example one may consider the
probability of a volcano erupting. This is not an experiment which can be performed multiple times
and as such one may approach this problem by expressing the probability in terms of a degree of belief
in the occurrence of that specific event, perhaps based upon prior information or data.
The two approaches also possess different viewpoints of what mathematical object can be considered
as random, hence yielding fundamental different perspectives of modelling. In the classical approach
unknown quantities such as model parameters are for instance considered to be fixed and ought to
be determined according to an estimator with the corresponding measures of uncertainty being found
by exempli gratia using a (often asymptotic) distributional assumption.
Contrary, in the Bayesian
approach; model parameters, latent states in exempli gratia a hidden Markov model (HMM) and the like
can be considered stochastic variables, hence implying that one can find the entire distribution rather
than just a point-estimate, thus enabling the measures of uncertainty to be expressed directly in terms
of this distribution. The significant difference between the two paradigms therefore mostly arise from
how they model problems. In the classical approach one often seeks the most likely outcome, while in
the Bayesian approach one is interested in finding the distribution of all outcomes [10].
This is the Bayesian approach or interpretation of probability. Note that there is no right or wrong
interpretation of probability, it is simply two different perspectives with each having their own respective
advantages, despite the fact that they both depend upon the same underlying mathematical foundation
and the same axiomatic representation [10].
C.1.1
BayesŌĆÖ Theorem
We shall now present the most fundamental theorem in the field of Bayesian statistics and inference,
known as BayesŌĆÖ theorem (or BayesŌĆÖ rule) which is a very simple yet exceptionally useful result that
describes how ones degree of belief in a probabilistic setting, ought to change on account of related
evidence becoming available [69].
Theorem C.1.1 (BayesŌĆÖ Theorem). Consider the stochastic variables A and B, then the conditional
probability density functions fulfill the following statement
p(A|B) = p(B|A)p(A)
p(B)
.
(C.1)
Proof. This follows directly from the product rule of probability, consider [10, 107]
p(A, B) = p(A|B)p(B) = p(B|A)p(A).
(C.2)
Dividing both sides of the equation by p(B) ╠Ė= 0 yields the desired outcome, namely BayesŌĆÖ theorem.
BayesŌĆÖ theorem is a widely applicable result in probability theory, hence not solely used in Bayesian
statistics. It provides a formula for finding p(A|B) given that p(B|A) is known, hence inverting the
conditional probability density function. Thus in a probabilistic setting of a model where the distribution
of a target is conditioned on a set of model parameters and an observation, it then enables one to find
the distribution of the model parameters given the data-set, which is the inference problem.
Remark. BayesŌĆÖ theorem establishes and provides the foundation of Bayesian statistics and inference.
==PAGE:108==
C.1 Bayesian Statistics
99
C.1.2
Bayesian Inference
LetŌĆÖs now introduce the inference problem. Consider to this end the setting of a probabilistic model, for
which a target y is assigned a distribution p(y|╬Č) conditional on a set of specified parameters ╬Č ŌłłRD,
where the conditional dependence on the observation here and in the following is not asserted explicitly.
Assume now that we have a data-set of targets D. Given a model as well as a data-set the inference
problem associates how (in the Bayesian setting) to find the distribution of the model parameters p(╬Č|D).
Consider then BayesŌĆÖ theorem [10, 29, 44, 56]
p(╬Č|D) = p(D|╬Č)p(╬Č)
p(D)
=
p(D|╬Č)p(╬Č)
R
RD p(D|╬Č)p(╬Č)d╬Č
(C.3)
We observe that BayesŌĆÖ theorem indeed provides a way to solve the inference problem. However, before
we go into further details letŌĆÖs briefly describe the most common terminology in Bayesian statistics.
Here p(╬Č) is known as the prior distribution of the parameters, which often depends on a set of hyper-
parameters (if this is the case; then equation C.3 ought to be adjusted accordingly). The prior distribu-
tion expresses the initial degree of belief before taking the evidence (the data-set) into account, whereas
p(╬Č|D) is referred to as the posterior distribution of the parameters after taking the evidence D into
account. We hereby see that BayesŌĆÖ theorem offers a method for updating ones degree of belief providing
that evidence is available. which can be applied recursively as data sequentially becomes available. The
function p(D|╬Č) is the likelihood function, which is also well known in the classical setting of statistics,
where the argument is often fixed and we let the condition be free to vary, hence in general not being
a proper distribution as a function of ╬Č. This provides the reason for introducing the normalisation
term p(D), which is the marginal likelihood due to being marginalised over the parameters, and it is
often referred to as the model evidence. This normalisation term is constant for a fixed data-set and
does not depend on the model parameters, hence why BayesŌĆÖ theorem is often expressed in terms of a
proportionality statement as it has been done in the following [10]
Posterior ŌłØLikelihood ├Ś Prior.
(C.4)
This means that in the Bayesian setting if one is only interested in a point-estimate of the parameters
(which is usually not the case) then one can find this without having to compute the normalisation term,
which is for instance what the maximum-a-posterior (MAP) method exploits, by solving the following
╦å╬ČMAP = arg sup
╬ČŌłłRD p(D|╬Č)p(╬Č).
(C.5)
Note for a uniform prior distribution that it coincides with the maximum likelihood estimation (MLE),
hence ╦å╬ČMAP = ╦å╬ČMLE. However, in the Bayesian setting we are interested in obtaining the entire posterior
distribution over the parameters, hence why other methods are necessary. The problem in the setting of
Bayesian inference is that the marginal likelihood does usually not yield analytical closed-form solutions
due to the integral in equation C.3 predominantly being intractable. The Bayesian inference problem
is therefore; how to identify the distribution over the parameters, which is directly solved by applying
BayesŌĆÖ theorem, although due to the integral in equation C.3 usually being intractable approximation
methods have to be considered. The often high dimensionality of Bayesian problems mean that classical
approaches such as quadratures are not ideal due to the curse of dimensionality, hence the need for
other approximation methods have to be considered, which will be done subsequently [10].
Before considering other approximation methods, letŌĆÖs briefly consider inference in classical statistics.
In classical statistics a standard approach is to make a point-estimate of exempli gratia the model
parameters using for instance the maximum likelihood method. Here as the name suggests we are inter-
ested in finding the set of parameters, which yield the maximum value of the likelihood function, hence
(see [104] for a more thorough and comprehensive explanation of the maximum likelihood principle).
╦å╬ČMLE = arg sup
╬ČŌłłRD p(D|╬Č).
(C.6)
==PAGE:109==
C.1 Bayesian Statistics
100
The maximum likelihood principle requires a distributional assumption, which depends on the model.
In order to perform maximum likelihood we first have to determine the likelihood function, which is
the joint (conditional) probability density function of all the targets in the data-set. For time series
data where there is a serial dependence structure of the targets we ought to exploit the conditional
independence, thus meaning that the likelihood function is generally given as follows [104]
p(D|╬Č) = p(y1, . . . , yn|╬Č) =
"
n
Y
t=p+1
p(yt|yp, . . . , ytŌłÆ1, ╬Č)
#
p(y1, . . . , yp|╬Č).
(C.7)
Notice that the number of targets necessary to condition on (here denoted by p) depends on the model.
A point-estimate of the parameters can hereby be found by optimisation of the appropriate problem.
Note here for independent (and identically distributed) targets that the likelihood function simplifies.
Moreover, for both maximum likelihood and maximum-a-posterior we often apply the logarithm to
ensure numerical stability of the corresponding optimisation algorithms. This is possible due to the
logarithm being a monotonically-increasing function, hence not changing the maxima.
The problem of Bayesian inference is thus a problem of approximating integrals, which is usually not
possible with standard numerical procedures such as quadratures, due to the often high dimensionality
of the problem [48]. In Bayesian statistics there is therefore other approximation methods, which are
customary. There is specifically three distinct paradigms popular in Bayesian inference [10, 29, 43].
Markov-Chain Monte-Carlo are stochastic methods for which an approximation is produced by
drawing samples for which these asymptotically yield the posterior distribution in question.
Variational Inference is a deterministic approximation method, which provided a family of simpler
distributions attempts to find the distribution yielding the best fit to the posterior, according
to the Kullback-Leibler divergence. Thus converting the inference problem into an optimisation
problem.
Laplace Approximation is an application of LaplaceŌĆÖs method, where a Gaussian distribution that is
centered at the maximum-a-posterior estimate is used to approximate the posterior distribution.
These paradigms each provide a different approach to Bayesian inference and each have their respective
advantages and disadvantages corresponding to for instance the quality of the approximation, scaling as
well as time complexity, hence the appropriate paradigm may depend upon the problem being modelled.
We will restrict the discussion to Laplace approximation (LA), which we shall introduce subsequently.
C.1.3
Laplace Approximation
Consider the aforementioned problem of Bayesian inference presented in equation C.3. The object of
interest is to approximate the otherwise intractable posterior distribution. This is here done by using
a (multivariate) Gaussian distribution centered at the maximum-a-posterior [29]. Thus providing the
advantage of being computational fast as well as be a good approximation for a variety of problems
due to the Bernstein-Von Mises theorem, which states that; given certain conditions then the posterior
distribution will asymptotically be a (multivariate) Gaussian distribution [14]. Despite this it is still
an approximation leading to poor performance for problems such as for instance multi-modal posterior
distributions, while also having the disadvantage of a limited focus on global properties of the posterior
distribution. LetŌĆÖs now examine the origin of this approximation, we are interested in approximating
the intractable posterior distribution using BayesŌĆÖ theorem. We note here that

C.1.4
Predictions
In statistics and machine learning it is often of interest to develop a model to enable the prediction of
targets with measures of uncertainty based upon current and past information. We will here present
a short and concise introduction. We will start with the classical approach and then describe how to
make predictions in the Bayesian setting. We shall begin by introducing a fundamental result, which
states how to obtain the optimal prediction. To establish this we first introduce the following [86].

distribution [92]. However, this does not yield a unique interval, hence an additional requirement is
necessary in order to obtain a unique interval. One approach is to choose a central interval such that
each tail contains (1 ŌłÆ╬▒)/2 of the posterior distribution [92]. The credible interval thus constitutes the
probability of the true parameter or true value being within the specified interval.
Probability distributions comprise a fundamental aspect in Bayesian statistics, particularly in relation
to prior distributions, and shall therefore subsequently be reviewed in further details.

C.2 Stochastic Processes
103
C.1.5
Prior Distributions
In Bayesian statistics prior distributions constitute a vital contribution towards the success of the
acquired results [41]. Therefore, in order to conduct a sensible analysis, a moderate understanding of
prior distributions ought to be appropriate. We shall here introduce three types of prior distributions.
Non-Informative prior distributions express definite information of a variable (general).
Weakly-Informative prior distributions express partial information of a variable (class of problems).
Informative prior distributions express general or vague information of a variable (problem specific).
A particular favorable class of priors in Bayesian statistics are conjugate priors. A prior is conjugate
to a likelihood function if it is a probability distribution, which yields a posterior distribution within
the same family of probability distributions as the prior distribution, hereby producing a closed-form
expression for the posterior distribution. Note for any likelihood function within the exponential family
(for more information please resort to [104]) that there exists a conjugate prior [10].
The reader is referred to [10, 41] for a more thorough review and explanation of prior distributions.
C.2
Stochastic Processes
A time series is a realisation of a stochastic process and the corresponding analysis can therefore be
considered as the modeling of stochastic processes, hence the reason for providing a brief introduction
to this otherwise comprehensive topic within probability theory [86]. A stochastic process is defined as
a collection of stochastic variables specified according to an index set T , which in time series analysis
is often either continuous time {y(t), t ŌłłR}, discrete time {yt, t ŌłłZ} or a subset of these. Stochastic
processes are in analogy to stochastic variables characterised by their distribution. The distributional
specification of a stochastic processes is also similar to that of a multivariate stochastic variables, while
a stochastic process can be an infinite collection of stochastic variables and is therefore necessarily
restricted to a finite-dimensional distribution specified according to n ŌłłN yielding an n-dimensional
distribution [86, 145]. The family of all these distributions constitutes the family of finite-dimensional
distributions of the stochastic process, which completely characterises the stochastic process. A stochas-
tic process can also be characterised by its moments, although since its moments are (often) dependent
on time this results in moment functions rather than constant moments. An important moment function
for stochastic processes is the auto-covariance function defined as follows [86]
Definition C.2.1 (Auto-Covariance Function). Consider a stochastic process {yt} with mean function
┬Ąt, then its auto-covariance function is given by the following expression
╬│t1,t2 = Cov[yt1, yt2] = E[(yt1 ŌłÆ┬Ąt1)(yt2 ŌłÆ┬Ąt2)].
(C.25)
A special case is t1 = t2, which gives the variance function Žā2
t . Note that the second moment is
given by the auto-covariance function, hence the second order moment representation of the process
is constituted of the mean function and the auto-covariance function. Similar to the auto-covariance
function we can define the auto-correlation function (ACF) correspondingly
Žüt1,t2 =
╬│t1,t2
q

(C.26)
It is feasible to condition the auto-correlation on the targets in-between the two time steps, which is
referred to as the partial auto-correlation function (PACF) is defined accordingly
Definition C.2.2 (Partial Auto-Correlation function). Consider a stochastic process {yt}, then the
partial auto-correlation function is given by the following expression
╬Št1,t2 = Corr[yt1, yt2|yt2ŌłÆ1, . . . , yt1+1].
(C.27)
==PAGE:113==
C.2 Stochastic Processes
104
A useful concept in time series analysis is the characteristic of a stationary stochastic process, which
describes how a stochastic process develops through time. Two different definitions for stationarity of a
stochastic process are available, depending upon the degree to which the stochastic process is stationary,
these are known as strongly-stationary and weakly-stationary respectively [86].
Definition C.2.3 (Strongly-Stationary). A stochastic process is said to be strongly-stationary if all
finite-dimensional distributions are invariant to change in time.
Definition C.2.4 (Weakly-Stationary). A stochastic process is said to be weakly-stationary of order k
if all the first k moments are invariant to change in time.
Remark. A weakly-stationary process of order 2 is referred to as weakly-stationary or just stationary.
In time series analysis we are often satisfied with a stochastic process being weakly-stationary id est
a stochastic process where the mean and the variance are constant and the auto-covariance function
solely depends on the time difference, which we will specify by the lag k (in the following k ŌłłZ) [86].
In time series analysis it is common to only observe a single realisation, hence making it necessary
for an appropriate assumption in order to infer distributional characteristics of the stochastic process.
A su’¼Ćicient assumption is that the stationary process is ergodic, which in principle means that any
statistic of the stationary stochastic process can be established from a single realisation [86]. This is
often a general requirement, as we are often interested in designated statistics such as for instance the
second order moment representation (the reader is referred to [69] for more information). In practice
when observing a time series where the underlying stochastic process is unknown, the different statistics
such as aforementioned quantities have to be estimated. Assuming that the stochastic process {yt} is
ergodic (and therefore can be assumed stationary), the auto-covariance function can be estimated [86]

LetŌĆÖs now present an example of a stochastic process. The most fundamental stochastic process in
time series analysis, which will become evident by its frequent use throughout the following section, is
known as a white-noise process. A white-noise process is defined as follows [86].
Definition C.2.5 (White-Noise Process). A white-noise process is a stochastic process {ŽĄt}, consisting
of mutually uncorrelated identically distributed stochastic variables with E[ŽĄt] = 0 and Var[ŽĄt] = Žā2.
Remark. In many settings it is additionally required that the white-noise process is Gaussian distributed,
while it is also often mutually independent rather than simply uncorrelated.
