Another important stochastic process in time series analysis is a Markov process [86].
Definition C.2.6 (Markov Process). A Markov process is a stochastic process {yt} for which
p(ytn+1|ytn, . . . , yt1) = p(ytn+1|ytn).
(C.37)
Note that all information of the value at the next time step depends solely on the current value.
This is referred to as the Markov property; given the present, the future is independent of the past [145].
Remark. A Markov process is in the literature sometimes referred to as a Markov chain.
Finally, in time series analysis a lot of analytical consideration can be simplified by the use of
operators. There is primarily two operators which we will make use of, namely the backwards-shift
operator B and the backwards difference operator Ōłć. Consider a stochastic process {yt}, then the
operators are defined as follows (note that these operators are indeed linear) [86]
Byt = ytŌłÆ1,
(C.38)
Ōłćyt = (1 ŌłÆB)yt = yt ŌłÆytŌłÆ1.
(C.39)
The reader is referred to [69, 86, 145] for more information regarding stochastic processes.
C.3
Linear Processes
The most elementary approach to time series analysis is to consider linear models, as these are simple
and provide for easy interpretation, while they often produce useful results as well as insight into the
system dynamics. We will here briefly introduce the most frequently applied linear models. However,
prior to that letŌĆÖs shortly, yet precise, describe the features of a linear process in more general terms.
In general a linear process can be interpreted as the output of a linear system where the input is a
white-noise process and is explicitly defined as follows [86].
Definition C.3.1 (Linear Process). The process {yt} given by
yt = ┬Ą +
X

(C.40)
where {ŽĄt} is a white-noise process and ┬Ą is the mean of the process, is called a (general) linear process.

We are now ready to describe the most common linear models studied in time series analysis.
C.3.1
Auto-Regressive Model
The first class of linear models, which we will consider is the auto-regressive models. An auto-regressive
model of order p is defined as follows [140].
Definition C.3.2 (Auto-Regressive Process). The process {yt} given by
yt + ŽĢ1ytŌłÆ1 + ┬Ę ┬Ę ┬Ę + ŽĢpytŌłÆp = ┬Ą + ŽĄt,

The current value is here modelled by a linear combination of the values at previous time steps
and the white-noise error at that current time step (and the mean of the process). An auto-regressive
process is always invertible and stationary if all the roots of the corresponding characteristic polynomial
ŽĢ(zŌłÆ1) are within the unit circle [86].
C.3.2
Moving-Average Models
The second class of linear models, which we will describe is referred to as the moving-average models.
A moving-average model of order q is defined as follows [140].
Definition C.3.3 (Moving-Average Process). The process {yt} given by

The current value is here modelled by a linear combination of the white-noise error terms at the

roots of the corresponding characteristic polynomial ╬Ė(zŌłÆ1) are within the unit circle [86].

C.3.4
Auto-Regressive Integrated Moving-Average Models
We will now present a different class of linear models, which differ from the previous linear models
by being non-stationary. In practical applications one often has to deal with time series which cannot
be described by a stationary process due to for instance long-term trends, periodic trends or more
general time-varying behavior [86]. The long-term trends can however often be removed by a suitable
differencing, such that the time series can be adequately described by a stationary ARMA(p,q) process,
hence obtaining a simple model for the non-stationary time series. This class of linear models is referred
to as auto-regressive integrated moving-average models. An auto-regressive integrated moving-average
model of order p, d and q is defined as follows [86].
Definition C.3.5 (Auto-Regressive Integrated Moving-Average Process). The process {yt} given by
ŽĢ(B)Ōłćdyt = ╬Ė(B)ŽĄt,
(C.49)
where {ŽĄt} is a white-noise process This is called an auto-regressive integrated moving-average process
of order p, d and q or simply an ARIMA(p,d,q) process. The corresponding characteristic polynomials
ŽĢ(zŌłÆ1) and ╬Ė(zŌłÆ1) are of order p and q respectively, with roots inside the unit circle.
The auto-regressive Integrated moving-average models, as the name implies, generalise both the
auto-regressive models, the moving-average models and the auto-regressive moving-average models.
Remark. These models are sometimes collectively referred to as the Box-Jenkin models on account of
being popularised by said authors in the book ŌĆØTime Series Analysis: Forecasting and ControlŌĆØ.
==PAGE:117==
C.3 Linear Processes
108
C.3.5
Identification
In applications the underlying stochastic process producing the data is unknown, hence we ought to both
identify the type of process generating the data as well as find appropriate estimates of its parameters.
We will here describe how this can be done for the linear models presented throughout this section. We
have already presented how to acquire estimates of the parameters and we will therefore now start by
introducing how the presented linear processes can be identified. When discussing the identification of
linear processes based on observed time series the most vital tools are the auto-correlation function and
the partial auto-correlation function, as these are paramount for providing insight into the stochastic
nature of the process. Note that model building, when trying to identify the generating process, is
an iterative process where the identification process often has to be repeated after performing model
verification, in order to acquire are suitable model structure to describe the data.
Assume now that we have observed a time series, which is not necessarily stationary or perhaps its
characteristic polynomial has a root close to the unit circle, which implies a slow decrease towards zero
of the auto-correlation function. This can also be the case if the time series has slow variation compared
to the sample-rate. It is recommended that this behaviour is removed through for instance, differencing
[86]. We are interested in finding the lowest order of differencing d yielding a su’¼Ćiciently fast decrease
towards zero, which in practice is often 0 (in the case of stationarity), 1 or 2.
Assume that we now have a stationary time series, either from a stationary process or by choosing a
suitable differencing d. Then we are able describe the process by an ARMA(p,q) process, where we will
have to identify the order of p and q. This can be done by choosing p and q such that the estimated auto-
correlation and partial auto-correlation functions which best approximate the corresponding theoretical
functions. For the assessment of this the characteristics of the theoretical function ought to be considered
and an overview of these characteristics can be found in table C.1 for each of the linear processes.
Table C.1: Identification of Linear Processes.
Model
ACF (Žük)
PACF (╬Škk)

a pure moving-average process is appropriate. For an AR(p) process it holds that [86]

C.3.6
State-Space Representation
These linear processes can also be represented by a broader and more flexible class of models known
as state-space models. In a physical setting the evolution of the system dynamics is often specified by
latent states. The object of interest; the latent states, can not be observed directly but are associated
to measurable targets specified according to a state-space model, hence enabling the inference of the
latent states from the measurable targets [35]. This further enables the modelling of both the latent
states as well as the measurable targets being influenced by noise disturbances.
The general form of a linear state-space model (in discrete time) is the following [86]
xt = AtxtŌłÆ1 + BtutŌłÆ1 + ŽĄt
(state equation),
(C.52)
yt = Ctxt + ╬Įt
(measurement equation),
(C.53)
where the process {xt} is M-dimensional latent states (notice that this is in fact a Markov process),
ut ŌłłRN are external deterministic inputs, while the process {yt} is E-dimensional measurable targets
and At ŌłłRM├ŚM, Bt ŌłłRM├ŚN, Ct ŌłłRE├ŚM are coe’¼Ćicients matrices, which here can depend on time
even though they are often time-invariant. The processes {ŽĄt} and {╬Įt} are mutually uncorrelated,
with zero-mean vector and serially uncorrelated covariance matrices ╬ŻŽĄ,t and ╬Ż╬Į,t [86]. Note that the
state equation reflects the development of the dynamics for the latent states, whereas the measurement
equation describes the measurable targets and their relation to the latent states.
State-space models hereby gives rise to a variety of models, especially models of physical problems,
although as previously stated the aforementioned linear processes can also be represented as state-space
models and to illustrate this, letŌĆÖs present a short example. Consider an ARMA(1,1) process, as follows
yt + ŽĢ1ytŌłÆ1 = ŽĄt + ╬Ė1ŽĄtŌłÆ1,
(C.54)
where {ŽĄt} is a white-noise process with variance Žā2. This can now be written in state-space form

C.3.7
Model Selection
It is often customary to apply different models, hence making it reasonable to define measures for
comparing and evaluating the performance of these, in order to establish and select the best model.
Here classical criteria from statistics will be defined, which adhere to the use of maximum likelihood.
One approach for selecting the best performing model and one which also enables the comparison
of non-nested models is to use an information criteria. Here we will consider three information criteria.
Definition C.3.6 (AkaikeŌĆÖs Information Criteria). AkiakeŌĆÖs information criteria is defined as follows
AIC = ŌłÆ2 log p(D|╦å╬ČMLE) + 2D,
(C.57)
where ╦å╬ČMLE ŌłłRD, hence D denotes the number of parameters, while D denotes the the data-set of targets.
Note again that the conditional dependence on the observations here has been suppressed.
Note that the objective when comparing models using an information criteria is to obtain the
minimum value of the appropriate information criteria. Notice that AkaikeŌĆÖs information criteria enables
one to select the model with the largest value of the maximum likelihood while avoiding overfitting.
However, when a lot of examples are available it has a tendency to accept too many parameters [86].
Therefore an alternative information criteria is proposed [92].
==PAGE:119==
C.4 Neural-Networks
110
Definition C.3.7 (Bayesian Information Criteria). The Bayesian information criteria is defined as
BIC = ŌłÆ2 log p(D|╦å╬ČMLE) + D log n,
(C.58)
where n is the number of examples.
Notice that the Bayesian information criteria takes into account the number of examples which
are available and penalises the number of parameters according to (the logarithm of) this contrary to
AkaikeŌĆÖs information criteria, hereby ensuring that overfitting is avoided even with many examples.
C.4
Neural-Networks
The field of deep learning within machine learning is composed of neural-networks (NN), a popular
class of non-linear black-box models that, as the name suggests, are based upon how biological neural-
networks process information [56]. These are highly flexible due to being non-linear and have as a
consequence become very popular within many fields of science where they have been used to facilitate
the development of new and surpassing analyses and results within artificial intelligence [9]. Therefore
constituting and indispensable part of machine learning, hence the reason for this particular section.
There exists numerous types of models within the broader class of models referred to as neural-networks,
although we shall here restrict the discussion to comprise of feed-forward neural-networks (FFNN).
C.4.1
Feed-Forward Neural-Networks
The simplest type of neural-network is a feed-forward neural-network, which consists of a set of in-
formation processing units (often referred to as neurons) organised in a layers-wise network coupled
by weighted connections. The information is then processed layer-wise imitating the behaviour of its
biological counterpart. LetŌĆÖs now describe a feed-forward neural-network in further details; it consists
of an input-layer with the number of input-units being equal to the dimension of the observations such
that unit i is given an activation equal to xi. This initial activation is then propagated forward, often in
a fully-connected (meaning that each unit is coupled to each other unit in the subsequent layer) manner
to a so-called hidden-layer with a positive number of hidden-units, which are then transformed using
a non-linear activation function and then potentially propagated forward again if there are multiple
hidden-layers (in which case the neural-network is referred to as a deep) and at last the activation is
propagated forward to the output-layer where the number of output-units is specified by the dimension
of the targets or the number of classes, depending on whether it is a regression or a classification task.
This procedure is known as a forward-pass [56]. For an overview please refer to figure C.1.
==PAGE:120==
C.4 Neural-Networks
111
Figure C.1: An example of the architecture of a deep feed-forward neural-network.
Remark. Neural-networks were originally proposed by Warren S. McCulloch and Walter Pitts in their
work: A Logical Calculus of the Ideas Immanent in Nervous Activity, from 1943.
Mathematically a feed-forward neural-network is simply a mapping. A formal description follows;
in a neural-network the initial activation is first constructed from an observation x ŌłłRp and then

C.4.2
Back-Propagation
LetŌĆÖs proceed the discussion by introducing how to train a feed-forward neural-network. The training
of a feed-forward neural-network is a supervised learning problem where; given a data-set D we are
==PAGE:122==
C.4 Neural-Networks
113
interested in finding the most optimal weights and biases w in regards to the specified objective function.
This is conventionally done with the use of gradient descent algorithms, hence the requirement for the
gradients, which is here realised with the use of back-propagation. Back-propagation is an algorithm for
computing the gradients of a feed-forward neural-network to ensure that the prescribed gradient descent
algorithms are indeed applicable, and shall subsequently be described in further details. Consider an
objective function J(w, D) and the associated problem of computing the derivative with respect to wij.

that the activation function is indeed differentiable. However, note that this is not the case for exempli

be acquired by defining the derivative at x = 0 to be either 0 or 1. It is hereby seen from equation C.70
that the value of ╬┤ for a particular unit is attained by back-propagating the values of ╬┤ from units in
subsequent layers, hence the origin of the name. Furthermore, please note in equation C.70 that the
==PAGE:123==
C.4 Neural-Networks
114
sum is with reference to the second index of the weights thus representing back-propagation, whereas
in the forward-pass the sum is with respect to the first index [10]. The values of ╬┤ for the output-units
can be found straightforward, since ak = ╦åyk (in the case of the identity activation function) and the
rest can then be found by recursion, leading to the computing of the gradient, hence enabling the
use of gradient descent algorithms and the training of the feed-forward neural-network. This can be
generalised such that each layer has a specific activation function as well as methods using (mini)batches
such as stochastic gradient descent [10].
Figure C.3: Illustration of back-propagation, specifically the computation of ╬┤j.
The back-propagation algorithm can also be applied in a similar approach to compute higher-order
derivatives such as the Jacobian and the Hessian to enable more complex optimisation algorithms [10].
Furthermore, note here that the back-propagation algorithm is in contrast to numerical approximation
methods such as for instance finite-difference methods (please refer to [10] for more information).
C.4.3
Practical Considerations
LetŌĆÖs now present a selection of practical methodologies key to the success of feed-forward neural-
networks. In the practical setting of training a feed-forward neural-network it is necessary to initialise
the weights and biases, for which several strategies are available. The initialisation of the weights and
biases is of importance due to the vital effect on the success of the training and thus also the results.
It is often standard practice to initialise the biases as constants, regularly zero or close to zero, and
the weights as samples from either a uniform or a Gaussian distribution [48]. A popular choice for the
weights of a layer with W ŌłłN units, fully-connected to a layer with A ŌłłN units, is the following [47]

It is common practice to choose a sensible input representation of the observations to ensure good
performance, hence the reason for regularly applying the procedure of standardisation [50]. Here the
observations are standardised such that the training-set has zero-mean and unit standard deviation,
as this procedure does not alter the information but rather improves the performance by for instance
==PAGE:124==
C.4 Neural-Networks

transforming the observations into a range more suitable for the activation functions [77]. The stan-

This procedure is referred to as batch-normalisation and was proposed in [62] (alternatively confer [28]).
Using these characteristics it is possible to train a feed-forward neural-network by completing a forward-
pass and then using back-propagation, for each example in the data-set. This procedure is referred to
as an epoch, and is in practice executed multiple times in order to train and improve the performance,
by permitting the neural-network to learn from the data-set multiple times to ensure that an adequate
minima is found, as the objective function has numerous local minima due to for instance weights being
permutable within layers, which is known as weight space symmetry [48]. Note that it is often customary
to shuffle the data-set (except for time series data) at each epoch to achieve greater generalisation [95].
Please refer to the following two articles for more in depth discussions on the use of epochs [70, 153].
An often applied practical and simple approach to enhance the performance of a feed-forward neural-
network is to employ the general strategy of ensemble methods, which refers to the technique of separately
training an assorted number of models and then combining these by model averaging [48]. The reason
for this improving the performance, is due to the models oftentimes not making the same errors on
the test-set on account of, for instance different initialisations, (mini)batches, et cetera. For illustrative
purposes, letŌĆÖs present an example. Consider a set of k ŌłłN different regression models and suppose
==PAGE:126==
C.4 Neural-Networks
117
that model i produces the error ŽĄij for example j, where {ŽĄij} for fixed i are white-noise processes with
constant variances v, which are mutually correlated in the sense that Cov[ŽĄij, ŽĄlj] = c. This means that
the error corresponding to the average prediction of the ensemble of models is 1
k
Pk
i=1 ŽĄij, hence [48]

c,
(C.90)
where the second equality follows from the linearity of the expectation. It hereby follows, that if the
errors are perfectly correlated id est c = v, the expected squared error reduces to v indicating no
advantage of using the ensemble, whereas in the case of the errors being completely uncorrelated id est
c = 0, then the expected squared errors is 1
kv. This means that the performance attained from using
an ensemble of models will on average perform at least as well as any given model in the ensemble and
will improve if the errors between any pair of models differ from that of perfect correlation.
There exists different ensemble methods, each constructing the ensemble of models differently [48].
For instance, bagging constructs k different data-sets, each of the same size as the original data-set, but
with each data-set being assembled using uniform sampling with replacement from the original data-set.
This means that the k data-sets differ due to missing examples and duplicates, and model i is then
trained using data-set i resulting in differences between the different models.
LetŌĆÖs now present some popular regularisation methods, which apply to feed-forward neural-networks.
The first regularisation method we consider is early-stopping, which is used in order to prevent overfitting.
This method, as the name suggests, simply consists of stopping the training when the training-errors
and validation-errors have failed to decrease in a specified number of epochs. The training-errors and
validation-errors typically decrease during training until a certain number of epochs upon which the
validation-errors begins to increase while the training-errors continues to decrease thus demonstrating
overfitting. The final model often uses the weights minimising the validation-errors [50].
Another standard approach to the regularisation of feed-forward neural-networks is dropout [141].
This method employs the strategy of randomly dropping units alongside the connections from the feed-
forward neural-network, hereby simulating different architectures during training to prevent overfitting.
This can be implemented layer-wise, where the choice of unit is random; in the simplest case, each unit
is assigned a fixed probability p for being retained. Here standard-values of p is often close to 1 for input-
units and often close to 0.5 for hidden-units, as proposed by [141], although this may vary according
to the specific problem and model. The application of dropout amounts to sampling a thinned neural-
network from the original feed-forward neural-network, where the thinned neural-network consists of
the units being retained. A feed-forward neural-network with n (dropout) units therefore leads to 2n
different combinations of thinned neural-networks. Training thus constitutes the sampling and training
of thinned neural-network for each example, hence implying that the training can be perceived as the
training of 2n thinned neural-networks with weight sharing, where each thinned neural-network is only
trained a few times if ever.
the weights corresponding to a unit being retained with probability p
during training are then rescaled during testing by multiplying with p yielding an outcome similar
to that of model averaging. Thus ensuring that the expected output under the dropout distribution
during training is equal to the actual output during testing [141].
The scaling hereby enables the
thinned neural-networks with shared weights to be combined into a single feed-forward neural-network
for testing [141]. In practice the dropout procedure is accomplished by sampling a Bernoulli variable
rj
iid
Ōł╝Bernoulli(p),
(C.91)
which is multiplied with the appropriate transformed activations to create a thinned neural-network
╦£zj = zj ┬Ę rj.
(C.92)
==PAGE:127==
C.4 Neural-Networks
118
Note that training (using for instance stochastic gradient descent) is done by sampling and training
a thinned neural-network for each example in a (mini)batch, where the forward-pass and the back-
propagation for that example is only done on that thinned neural-network. The gradients are then
averaged within each (mini)batch, where weights excluded due to dropout yield a contribution of zero.
The reader is referred to the instances [10, 48, 56], for a more thorough explanation of neural-networks.
